RATING: 4
People talk about how they want a replacement or re-implementation of Arkham Horror and the common answer is Eldritch. I think that's incorrect for a series of reasons, but mostly because MoM2e fits much better in terms of why people don't like EH as a replacement (it's a fine game, but it's different). MoM2e requires you to engage monsters, there are more consequences, the tension ratchets up as folks become deranged or damaged since there is no easy/consistent way of healing. The Mythos phase is something everyone fears much more. It's much more confined and almost claustrophobic in feel compared to EH's more worldly romp. In short, it's a Lovecrafting experience more so than EH is in many ways. The app is rather slick, even if you do spend a lot of time "heads down" in the app instead of engaged in the board or with other players.

I think some things really annoy me about the game though, the inability to heal is thematic, but an annoyance. That I get pegged by the Mythos phase *and* have to do a horror check against critters makes sense, but sort of sucks as you spend all of this time beating on stuff just to avoid gaining gobs of horror. I sort of regret basing the minis, but really, this is a game where the minis are super clunky but sweet (and helpful in terms of narrative as they are imposing with bases, and it makes the rooms crowded) to look at on the board... A real storage solution would help, I guess if I was willing to dedicate a suitcase or a ginormous wad of plick-n-pluck and build something... Last, even for an Ameritrash-style game, it's on rails courtesy of the scenario-driven system and as such, once you play through a scenario two, maybe three times, it falls flat (e.g. you know you have X turns until a critter spawns here, and you know what information an NPC is going to give you).

For a thematic experience though, it's hard to beat, but I come away from playing with a sour taste in my mouth for some reason, I don't know. I waffle on a 4 or 5; I want to play it again, but only scenarios I haven't seen (and there is a finite number) and then move on. That twist is because the emotional response is tied to the very thing that it excels at (theme and surprise), it ultimately falters with repeated play...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
From a mechanical perspective, there really isn't much in the way of meaningful decisions to make in the game, and some of the scenarios are loooong. 

But on the other hand, it's one of the most engaging storytelling games I've played, and doesn't *feel* anywhere near as long as Arkham / Eldritch even though it often is. 

The cost of buying not only the game, but enough scenarios to keep it interesting, is also fairly outrageous - and fortunately a burden I don't have to bear.

A mixed bag which overall I seem to enjoy. Will become a staple part of my case for being an Omnigamer...

RATING: 6
This is a fun coop game that uses an app to act as the gamemaster, revealing the layout of the mansion and the monsters/creatures/characters you encounter. The app is a nice touch in a lot of ways, in that it frees everybody up to be players, but it ends up absorbing or requiring a lot of focus. I find that the players all tend to crowd around the iPad reading the notes that the app issues. Perhaps if the app vocalized all of the notes instead, so the players just listened to it and reacted instead of obsessing over the iPad, it would solve my problem, but as it is now I found the app use to distract from the game.

RATING: 9
This game is quickly becoming one of my favorite boardgames, It is incredibly thematic and the integration with the app is extremely well done. I own and enjoyed the original Mansions of Madness, and while I do sometimes miss the ability to play against someone who can make intelligent decisions against you based on the board state, I still feel that what it adds makes it a much better game. Top among them is the fact that that you can no longer see all of the map and instead have to explore it as you go. This small change makes it more thematic, adds surprise to the game, and reduces dramatically the setup time.
The game is simple enough that even people playing it for the first time will quickly understand it, while still letting you make relevant decisions.

RATING: 8.5
Mansions of Madness 2nd Edition is a narrative-based dungeon crawler that has marked ups and downs. It should be compared to pandemic legacy for its narrative and to arkham horror lcg for its theme. Yet it has an app that the others don't have and others shouldn't have. The inclusion of an app adds more than it detracts from, and it works well for this kind of game.

Games that appeal to certain similar mechanics or themes and will be compared to during the review: Gloomhaven, Arkham Horror LCG, Pandemic Legacy.

Pros:
+Very easy to teach, on par with party games. Only the game owner needs to know the rules and have played at least once or twice. Then, they can teach as mechanics arise. I have always begun playing within 5 minutes of opening the game box, as all they need to know is they have 2 actions than can be used to move or interact to start playing. The difficulty of the first game depends on the player, and I've had successful games with new players starting at difficulties 2, 4 and 5 out of 5. So far all new players have lost (as a group) but they enjoyed the game. One new player even turned insane and won.
+Appealing narrative, it is like playing an interactive story, or reading a book, as long as you approach it with that in mind.
+The app sets up the mood with music, cutscenes and voice acting on the cutscenes. Don't listen to purists and actually try this game before bashing on it just for needing an app. The game is greatly enhanced by having an app rather than not.
+It plays very differently at all player counts, and each provide a different and good experience. Solo play is like reading a book, and is fully cooperative. With two players, you may keep only the 1+ insanities to keep it fully cooperative, or 2+ to include some annoying stuff. With 3 to 5 players, you have almost always someone or someones turn insane, and now you won't fully trust them. Even though the game is easier with more, as they turn insane, they start burning, pushing, stealing from each other, wanting to trade dynamites, not wanting to trade dynamites, and it is a different experience, and still fun. It might be best to remove the 1+ cards from 4 player games, and the 2+ too for 5 player games. This would balance the fact that people find it easier with more people.

Cons:
-Little bang for the buck (although once reaches a critical mass of $200 worth of product, it keeps adding more replayability with each extra expansion).
-Like all FFG's games, you need expansions to make the game worth it. The base game has four scenarios with low replayability. Expansions not only add scenarios, but make every scenario more replayable by adding more cards, investigators, monsters and tiles.
-Fiddly game, where you will spend a lot of the actual game play not playing, but either reading or placing tokens or finding cards or tiles.
-You don't mind investigator balance. Skill points sum to 21, but strength and will are the most useful ones on most characters in the party, except the caster, which needs lore instead of strength. Observation, Agility, and specially Influence, are weaker relative to the others. Ability power level between investigators has a lot of variance. Even if agility is the most common combat check, it won't be used as often because starting with a heavy weapon is more common than starting with a bladed one.
-From three to five players you will have a lot of downtime between player turns. If anyone doesn't like to read or follow the narrative, they will quickly become bored.
-The monster and base fit is bad, and some will hold tight but others will fall down all the time. There are residues in both the miniature peg that goes to the base and inside the base itself so that the cardboard inserts will get small damage indents.
-Spells. I don't get why they don't get the attack treatment and just resolve inside the app. It is annoying to randomly assign one card from a 5 card deck, including one you just saw.
-Most skill checks. I don't know why they didn't just hide the pass and fail effects, like the checks when interacting with search tokens and people. It is unthematic to know what will happen if you pass or fail, so there is no suspense or risk in spending clues.
-Player count balance: Two players have just too little actions to complete the most difficult missions. Three is the minimum to complete 4 and 5 star difficulties with a significant chance of success. The betrayer mechanic also doesn't scale too well. Betrayers appear on 4 cards that are used on 3+ players. However, becoming a betrayer on a three player game is too hard to overcome, while on a five player game it would be too weak.

Mixed:
~The miniatures. The plastic can hold a lot of detail, yet only the deep one hybrid has enough detail to make the most of the plastic. I would recommend the developers to include more detail in the future miniatures. The bad thing is that the
size tolerances aren't good and the (I assume manual assembly) for big pieces like the star spawn makes some stuff not regular. In my game, one star spawn can fit in
a tight box, and another can't because the wings and legs are glued with a different rotation.
~Buy in cost is actually $200: The core game itself, even though it comes with a lot of components, won't be able to offer too many playthroughs. The core only (with no expansions) scenarios will spawn the same monsters in the same places, you will get the same starting items often, you will get few to no map layout variations and the difficulty distribution is awkward (2 stars, 3 stars but takes 4-6 hours, 4 stars, and five stars). This means that after a few tries you will want something more than the two star scenario, but find that you have no options for smooth progression. There is one 3 star scenario with acceptable playtime in one expansion, and another expansion has a two and a four star scenario. Unfortunately, the DLC that only requires the base game is also four star difficulty. The upside is that once you get enough expansions, you will have enough monster, item, investigator and scenario variability to make it replayable enough that it is worth $200. With the core costing about $80 when it is available, it doesn't offer $80 worth of replayability, but something closer to $50. At $200 you get about $200 replayability. Note that as more expansions come, the replayability value will surpass its cost, making each expansion even more worthwhile. The advantage is that expansion monsters and cards are almost always used in every scenario. Also, new expansions not only offer new tiles for the new maps, but some are also included as new layouts for previous scenarios, increasing the replayability of existing ones.

You should get if:
+You like reading books or playing interactive stories.
+You like the Cthulhu mythos
+You can afford spending about $200 for reaching enough replayability.

You should instead get other games if:
-You need a lot of replayability. Get arkham horror lcg. It has more replayability per dollar.
-You are on a budget (the base game, at $80, is not worth it. You must include expansion costs inside the initial purchase for the game to have a minimum of replayability, so add 3 expansions for $120). The buy in cost for the game technically becomes $200 this way. Get pandemic legacy, since it is $50 for the full experience.
-You don't like the Cthulhu theme. Get Gloomhaven instead.

What you should ignore:
~Any concerns for the app. It has integrated five attack decks and every monster attack and horror deck, and random starting items and random items that drop in the game or monsters that appear (from a specific pool), so it adds quite some variability in gameplay just there. It also adds music and voiceovers for a more thematic experience. You also get rid of the need for a keeper and the game owner (typically the keeper) doesn't have to spoil themselves the story before playing. It provides just too much to this game to pass it just for having an app.

Expansion recommendations:
If you decided to invest in Mansions, what should you get with $200?
1) Core box for $85 (get it while in print, not when out of print and far more expensive)
2) Recurring Nightmares for $45
3) Streets of Arkham for $45
4) Beyond the Treshold for $25

If you liked those and still want more, or if you want specific investigators, then get Suppressed Memories.

Up to the products released for 2017, the least important expansion is Suppressed Memories. Recurring Nightmares adds everything Suppressed does, but additionally you get 2 new map layouts for the core's Cycle of Eternity and Shattered Bonds. Every mansion-like map that uses core tiles is also suited for the mansion-like tiles from Recurring Nightmares. The only good thing Suppressed Memories adds is more monsters and more investigators and a 3 star difficulty scenario to bridge the gap from cycle of eternity to the next viable scenario.

Why I ranked expansions like i did?
For me, replayability in this game depends on the following factors:
-Investigator pool, with an exponentially increasing team composition options based on quantity. (most from figures and tiles collections)
-Scenario Quantity (most from small box and big box)
-Scenario Map Layout variety per scenario (most from figures and tiles collections)
-Available starting items (most from small and big box)
-Monsters spawning in game (most from figures and tiles collections)
-Items you can get in game (most from small and big box)
-Damage and Horror cards (only from small and big box)
-Conditions and insanity cards (only from small and big box)
-Puzzle variety (only from big box)
-Any new additional mechanic (only from big box)

Most people will tend to rank just scenario quantity, but as you can see, there is more to it than just that.

Final Thoughts:
The game has a lot of theme, enhanced by the app's music and voiceovers. It is ridiculously easy to teach, but is fiddly, expensive and not enough investigator and player count balance. If you have less than $200 lying around, get pandemic legacy or gloomhaven, both popular and highly ranked games. If you have $200 or more, and can set aside around $100 per year for the next 5 years, consider mansions or arkham horror lcg. Prioritizing bang for the buck, replayability and mechanics means you lean to arkham horror lcg. If you prioritize ease of teaching and immersive theme, go for mansions.

Notes: I have around 15 play sessions as of the last edit of this review, with player counts 1 to 4.

Solo score: 9/10. Player scaling will limit you to the easier scenarios if you want to play with only 2 investigators.
Duo score: 8.5/10: It is the same as playing by yourself. The game is still agile enough to maintain attention to all the narrative.
3 player score: 8/10: Hard to maintain focus through every piece of narrative, and players will scatter to focus on goals. The betrayer mechanic hits hard here, as you suddenly went to a 3 vs game to a 2vs 1+game.
4 player score: 8.5/10: It is possible for supports to play their role, as it is very likely one pair will investigate while two others do their own thing. Even though you won't pay attention to every bit of narrative, the betrayers don't hit as hard.
5 players: Haven't played at this count, but I imagine the downtime between player actions would be a bit too much, and the betrayer mechanic would be too diluted to matter too much.

Ideal player counts: 1, 2 and 4. Houserulings, even if it is just including or excluding some insanity cards, may make 3 players a better experience. Five players, opposite from three, would require stronger betrayers or chance to become one. I would suggest trating 3+ cards as 4+ ones, and creating new 3+ insanity cards. Also create 5+ if you somehow manage to find five people willing to have that much downtime, with stronger betrayer mechanics.

RATING: 9
J U E G A Z O!!
Otro juego que va directo a mi lista de favoritos!

Sobre la famosa rejugabilidad:

La primera vez que juegas una misión (la que sea) es una experiencia increíble. La sensación de no saber que hay al otro lado de una puerta, de si te conviene o no abrirla en estos momentos, de si debieras investigar la misteriosa pila de papeles antiguos que hay sobre el escritorio antes de abandonar la habitación perdiendo un tiempo valioso con ello... para los que nos gusta este tipo de juegos de aventuras es alucinante.

La segunda vez (y siguientes) que te juegas la misma misión el escenario no es exactamente el mismo y los eventos que se suceden cambian lo suficiente como para que no puedas aprenderte ninguna misión de memoria. Incluso conociendo la trama y los objetivos, sigue siendo un desafío que no tienes por que ganar. Se convierte en un juego más parecido a lo que estamos acostumbrados a ver y jugar en este género. Conoces tus objetivos y como debes conseguirlos, y lo que no sabes es como se desarrollará la partida a causa de eventos, combates y equipo encontrado.

Para mi es un juegazo en ambos casos.

RATING: 9
I really enjoy this big box of Cthulu.  It is really the game that I always wanted Arkham Horror to be.  Some scenarios are combat-heavy, some are more in the mold of something like Clue.  

Comparing this to something like Time Stories, TS has a much stronger narrative, but of course it lacks replay-ability.  The narratives aren't quite so well constructed here but this leaves room for variation within the same mission.  

Some people will baulk at the required App stuff but it doesn't irk me at all.  It still feels like a board game first and foremost.  

The only downside to me is the bases that come with the game.  One of the biggest mysteries in the game is how Fantasy Flight thought this was a good idea.  You have these big bulky plastic platforms that obscure the tile art more than needed and the figures dont really fit well when you try and attach them.  I ended up re-basing all my miniatures.

RATING: 4
I guess I was expecting too much from the storytelling aspect of the game, but for me this simply doesn't cut it. The stories felt underwhelming and basic, nothing that I'd still remember in a few months time.

The characterizations are paper thin: Bad guys are bad just because they need to be bad. Add in randomized events that keep kicking you for no reason, insanity effects that can end a multi-hour session in a total disappointment and you're in for a bad time. Some of the scenarios somehow manage to feel rushed while still being extremely long.

The puzzles are very simple and rudimentary as well. For the most part, the game is just collecting clues and battling monsters, which is not very satisfying. People searching for good Cthulhu stories should steer clear.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The second edition of Mansions of Madness introduces quite a few innovations, but the main difference is obviously the app-driven game: this has both positives (almost no set up time, completely cancels the chance of ruining a multiple-hour game due to mis-set up, greater feeling of exploration with the map generated during gameplay) and negatives (much more predictability in enemies' behavior, more generic events for the sake of adaptation to different situations).

The play experience is undoubtedly smooth and enjoyable, and quite atmospheric, even if the length of the scenarios tends to be on the longer side, and probably some are not so replayable.

In a short sentence: better than first edition, good game, even if it is not the definitive game in its genre.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Initial Impression: This time FFG added an app to their list of high quality, thematic components. Counter-intuitively, the app actually adds to the thematic immersion of the game, with its dark visuals and soundtrack playing in the background. The gameplay is fairly straightforward, as each player is allowed 2 actions on their turn, which includes movement, exploration, and interaction with people and objects. The cooperative aspect of the game is highlighted as the players strive to survive the threats and obstacles of the Chapter, while accomplishing the mission objectives. Unfortunately, there's no mechanism included which prevent alpha-gaming. The one thing that puzzles me with this game is that it could very well have been a full fledged tablet game rather than a boardgame, as the app takes care of almost everything. Still, the game system has lots of potential and is ripe for expansions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I both enjoy this game and find aspects of it disappointing (largely component related). Despite my conflicted opinion of this game, I did purchase some of the expansions for it. In view of that and my willingness to continue playing it despite its flaws, Mansions does appear to have some magic in it. 

I wouldn't have bought this game if I hadn't found it on sale for a good discount from MSRP. It's more a $60 game than a $100 game. 

A lot of the monsters do not fit into the provided bases. The problem is so pervasive that I may just use the counters instead of the miniatures.

***EDIT 2/26/17: So I've played it enough to overcome my resistance to crafts/hobby work, I am going to re-base these miniatures. The included black bases are just awful.

***Edit 3/7/17: New bases are much better. My wife really enjoys this game, so its rating keeps creeping up. I am enjoying the game more with every play through. Now we'll see what happens once we finish playing each scenario 3 times or so.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
While the game as a whole product is about a 5 for me (games that leave me cold), rating it specifically as a [-]board game[/-] cardboard-collection drops it to 1 (without the app it's not playable at all => not a game).

- - - - - - - - - -

This is my first board game with app integration, and I can only hope that this trend dies pretty fast or at least they learn how to get it right.
The ratio of board game to app is way off for my taste – I was not playing an app-enhanced board game, but a cardboard-enhanced iPad-app, which someone shelled out $100 for printed cardboard.
But they don't really make use of the advantages of the app. The variations in scenario 1 are quite straightforward and not numerous (*1) - it can't hold a candle even compared to a simple build-your-own-adventure-book(let).

So all I'm left with is another thematic board game in H.P. Lovecraft's universe with dice-based combat and uninspired generic stories (like almost all other Cthulhu-related stories in games).
Clearly a game I'm not in the target group.

[b]Edit:[/b]
I've been told that the second scenario provides a much more interesting story/experience.
But honestly I will only play the game again if the owner specifically requests it, otherwise I have zero desire to try it again.


[b]*1[/b] — I have played a friend's copy (he would most likely rate it 8 or even 9) and listened carefully when he told me about the variations he encountered (he played the first scenario 4 or 5 times with different people).
He encountered 2 different buildings, some other decisions in the small arc with the butler, and had a different spawn as the sidekick-threat.

RATING: 9.8
The story telling in this game is sooooo rich that any session stays on your mental hard disk much longer longer than your average board game, which is a great sign of a good story teller really.

One last remark: since the program is available on 4 different platforms, there is absolutely no risk of software support in 5 or 10 years time. I still have my 1980's boardgames from Avalon Hill with assisted computer software and Windows is running these things on emulators better than it ever was.

Heavy stuff in time played. Even the simplest scenario took me 3+ hours. But with more play the game surpassed almost everything I played in my life. Totally Impressed. 
One hint: after playing the Escape from Innsmouth scenario, read the novel of HP Lovecraft. This game is breathtaking as a story teller...
UPDATE: Escape from Innsmouth is not alone as being so close to the original Lovecraft novels. Other scenarios (except for the first one) are really extensions to existent stories.
A W E S O M E and far surpassing traditional boardgames.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
If you have played the first mansions of madness and experienced the frustrations of one person running the game and the baddies, erase that from your memory as much as possible, because the app driven second edition essentially erases these woes and allows for a unique, and engaging story of a game that is fairly accessible and even faster to setup than the first edition. The app also allows each and every game to be truly unique as the same scenario will still involve different rooms in different places, different puzzles, different monsters, and possibly a different ending. I'm not sure I will go for the super long missions (the longest of which is listed at 4-6 hours), but most missions I can see enjoying in almost an interactive story kind of a way. 

The app itself runs the game and bad guys, and it does so in a way that is very straight forward and intuitive, but still doesn't blur the line between board game and video game. By all means this is a board game still. Also the app does a good job of keeping track of things like monsters health and attempts at the various puzzles, and also allows for monsters movement and attacks to occur differently each round, making the game feel more thematic and preventing some of the analysis into the "perfect move" that can dry games like this out a bit. 

Excellent cooperative game, and I look forward to playing it again.

RATING: 5

I feel that the app should have more sound effects for monsters and much more varied music, perhaps even procedurally generated sequences to heighten tension.

The price of this game is extremely high and it isn't great value in my opinion. If I didn't already have first edition and the expansions I would've given this a miss entirely on the basis of prohibitive cost.

Having said all that I have had a blast with this game, it is simple enough to entice non gamers and has just enough meat on the bones for veterans too. The pricing structure for future scenarios will dictate whether this stays in my collection or not. 

*Now that the first full expansion and proper DLC has been released it is clear that FFG are going to ask a lot of money for not much in return. I thought Imperial Assault was expensive but at least that has replay value...

RATING: 9
Amazing. As someone who is still mostly opposed to apps in games, this totally works. The app is awesome: the interface is intuitive and easy to navigate, graphics are well done, and the music is of a very high quality and really generates an eerie atmosphere (I highly recommend hooking the app up to a large TV so everyone can see/hear what's going on). 

For me, no other game so successfully recreates the feel of Lovecraft's stories as this: from the mystery of his occult mythology to the general uncanny feelings of  helplessness and despair you get from the books, this game has it all. The price is high, and replayability is limited, but to me, it's worth every penny. My favorite Lovecraft game and the best game of 2016.

RATING: 7.7
This game. Not sure what's up with it because I simultaneously want to criticize it and extol its virtues from a mountain top. First of all, I feel like 70% of my play is interacting with the app. This is heightened in solo play because I can't pass the app off to another player. This means my memories of the game revolve around the iPad and that bothers me just a bit.

However, the game makes great use of the app to streamline play and pull surprises. The benefit of an electronic element such as this is that the game can pull the rug out from under you and surprise you in ways a player restricted by structure can't. I LOVE that and want to be hit with more of those moments.

Much of this game is going to come down to scenario design and I'm excited for the future. Right now, the game has enormous potential but it doesn't fully live up to it. It's enjoyable and a solid game, but I'm not sure it's worth the price tag for what you get in the base game.

RATING: 5
Don't buy this game for the figures - Cthulhu Wars beats it hands down there. The game requires an app but any notification from other apps knocks out the sound on both IOS and Android devices. 

We played the short introductory scenario - there are only 4 - and it took us 3 hours after an hour of set up rather than the indicated hour and a half. Rules lack clarity, and often I moved twice then waited a good while for my next turn while the app handled the monsters and demanded tests. It's heavy on app time rather than player choices. 

It feels more like a dungeon bash than Call of Cthulhu and by the end was an absurd parody of Lovecraft as the last character was chased out by two Hunting Horrors, two cultists and a Deep One.

The storyline was pretty good but basic Mythos fare. The components are average apart from the miniatures which are ok but have ugly bases and are a pain to assemble with counters you slot in that serve little purpose. Those ugly black bases are also bafflingly too big to fit well in the room spaces.

Overall a pretty mediocre game that might appeal to people who like Arkham Horror (I don't much), long games or spending  £80+ on a game that Hugh and Bec were desperate to end and I enjoyed but not as much as some others.

My advice is just play Call of Cthulhu the rpg or buy Betrayal at House on the Hill - similar theme but better game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Though this is not the prototypical game that I am in the market for my gf and I have been getting into the Arkham mythos and this is a well-themed variation that has much of the flavour and story I like in a self-contained "haunted house" type game. In many ways, the style of this reminds me of Betrayal at House on the Hill, but while the AH prototype is ultra-light, MoM is a medium light, still fairly fluffy in terms of options, but with more gamey decisions, stats, and the like to deal with.

The game itself is interacted with through both a tile-based map and mini system and the integral app that plays the role that used to be manned by an actual person in the first edition. Most turns involve exploring locations (through the app), attacking monsters (through the app) or the like.  You cannot play without the game components though, as the app only tracks what things there are to explore, what monsters are lurking around, and what is going on in the story. The physical components are used to show where the players are, what items they have, where the monsters specifically are, &c.

If you couldn't tell, the game is heavily dominated by the app interface though. Though I wouldn't like this to become the norm (and highly doubt that it ever will) for this particular game I think it works very well and after having played this second edition I have almost zero desire to ever play the first because I can see how having a CPU handle things like variable combat results based on the weapon type used (a card deck based thing in the 1st ed) are cool, but how incredibly fiddly and time consuming they would be with a card-based system.

As with most FFG stuff, you really need to be into the theme and atmosphere over the gameplay itself to get full value here. There aren't a terribly large number of tough decisions (but more than there are in Betrayal), but there is some great theme and I love the way that every interaction: attacking or evading monsters, taking horror checks, &c. are all thematic and narrated through the app. 

This isn't a game that I think I would want to play into the ground though, because I'm sure knowing the stories too well would make it less enjoyable, and also easier in some ways. I have replayed one of the scenarios, and though a few things changed in terms of the monsters and tiles (as I own the expansion stuff now and the app can use those) I still knew too much about what was going to happen and had to hold my tongue for the part of the group that hadn't played it. For the time being I'm enjoying it as a couples game and I'm sure it will get some regular playtime, but without additional purchases the relative lack of available scenarios is an impediment, especially if you group wants / needs specific things in terms of difficulty or expected game length. This can, of course, be rectified to a large degree by opening your wallet, but presently the majority of the physical expansions are light on scenarios and heavy on bits (the 2 tile sets being the worst in this, as they contain a whole bunch of tiles and monsters but only have 1 unique scenario and retail for $50 each), so if you play it for the story (which I do) then you will end up spending a fair bit of money to have more options.

Aside: great minis (honestly: all of the nonsense about how the minis don't look decent is just daft to me; but I don't normally play these types of games and don't expect the world, I have plenty of minis background from my GW days...), but I'm not crazy about how tough the tiles are to slot into the small bases and how some of the minis are prone to popping out of their bases. I hate using glue because of the interaction with the tile held in the base, but it may be necessary to make the game usable. I've strongly considered at least rebasing them, if not painting, but I'm not sure I have the time to invest given how many figures I own.

RATING: 8
MoM features most of the ingredients i usually don´t like in a game: An adventure luckbased game with a RPG flavour with a horror theme!

Yet, the master gamer role is here provided by a very good app that, not only saves time, but also adjusts the difficulty and consistency of the story according to the players progress.
The quality of the stories and the environments, the art design (both of the app as in the board), the music and the sound effects, all contribute to allow a great immersion with the theme and a great experience. 
However, there´s still, of course, some dependency on the dice and the players run through a preset story where they can only decide turn left or right, attack or evade, move or explore, etc.
The game also has some obvious problems of replayability (minimized by expansions) and is expensive...but is a heck of a game, that´s for sure!

RATING: 6
[Unknown] - 7.25 (10 plays)
This is one of my goto Solo games. I enjoy the app controlling one side of the story and attempting to discover the story instead of trying to outsmart some AI (that's saved for other humans). The game is very successful in that respect. Playing with other people feels a lot like watching someone read a book and then tell you about it.

The quality of the miniatures is pretty poor. The whole clunky base with monster chits looks like it was a kitchen sink design decision. I would have rather seen them just keep the monster chits and go with better minis for the investigators. The cardboard pieces and cards are very good and what I would expect from FFG. 

[5 Nov 2017] - 6 (14 plays)
It's a very interesting solo game as the computer takes the place of one of the players. That let's the game tell a compelling story. Despite this, I had to drop the rating. First, I've become less interested in solo gaming. Pulling a computer out on those occasions when I can get a group together isn't as fulfilling. Second, the game needs expansions in order to stay fresh. The cost of the latest expansion is more than I want to keep pumping into a gaming experience for myself. At the rate I'm spending on this game, I could buy a new game console and get the same number of plays out of a solo game.

[29 Dec 2017] - 6 (14 plays)
Sold this as it wasn't getting played and I didn't like the price point for the latest expansions. The quality of the miniatures was the worst part of this game. They were pretty bad and would require a ton of time to just get ok miniatures after painting. I didn't necessarily mind the app driven nature of the game but very often (e.g. during combat), the generic text would get a little tedious and I'd start skimming for numbers instead of reading the whole paragraph. The length of the game and the setup involved just made this a non-starter. I stopped playing after the new game shine wore off.

RATING: 4
I LOVE this game. I can't think of any other game that was so intense and immersive, with a gripping story, that made you wonder what would happen next, all the time. Unfortunately this game is a complete rip off. The MSRP for the core game is 100$ and it contains exactly four missions. That wouldnt be too bad, if those missions had the same replayabilty as the missions in the first edition. Unfortunately they don't. The story and the core elements of each mission stay exactly the same, there arent even branching story paths any more. If you are lucky and restart the mission, the layout of the map and the monsters that spawn vary (in some levels not even that is the case), but thats it. Unless you enjoy the game mechanic and the challenge of replaying the same level with different characters, there is no real replay value in most of the levels - especially if you are like me, and you are mostly in it for the story. The two digital DLCs are allright, but Asmodee/Heidelberger cant be arsed to translate them into German. What really blows my mind though, are the first two physical Expansions that bring back the tiles and the minis from the first edition core game and big expansions. They are around 50$ each and contain exactly ONE mission, thats 50$ for one ~2h mission, which is ridiculous for a boardgame. The 3rd Expansion (with new content) is way better with 2 Missions for arround 30-35$ and new components, but still is VERY pricey for what you are acctually getting. Translations for the 4th expansion, as always, havent even been announced 2-3 months after the US release. The app itself is pretty primitive as well. Hardly any options, annoying confirmation messages you cant get rid off and no multiple save games. While the app is supported on almost all digital plattforms there is no cross plattform support. So if you get digital DLC on Steam and want to play it on your Apple/ Android device you have to rebuy it again. VERY unimpressive app, especially if you look at Valkyre, a free and improved version, done by only a few guys. The game is still amazing but, Asmodee/FFG/Heidelberger REALLY have to improve the content prizing and the replayability of the missions, as well as improve the app. Which they most likely wont, because this game is a cash cow as it is....Unless you are rich or dont care about replaying the same story over and over again this game is really not worth the investment, if you are not made of money thought you could get easily get 3-4 games with 1000 times the replayability that this game has to offer
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.7
Sadly, this is a contender for the most overhyped game for me.  I mean... top 20?!  The game is far too reliant on the app, but it has to be.  The app does everything but moving minis, dice rolls, and drawing/taking cards.  In other words, the app is ALMOST a standalone digital game (and could be if FFG wanted it to be), but you need to pay $80 or so to get the rest.  

Gameplay... I mean... EVERYTHING is app and dice.  Set up how the app tells you.  Put tokens where the app tells you.  To investigate, press the token on the app and roll dice.  To explore, press the token on the app and roll dice.  To attack, press the monster on the app and roll dice.  To evade, press the monster on the app and roll dice.  After each mythos phase, resolve a horror check by following the instructions on the app and rolling dice.

There aren't many interesting decisions to make.  You need to explore and investigate everything.  

For a game with an app, I was shocked as to how fiddly it was.  Also, it's extremely easy to overlook important actions, abilities, conditions, etc... given how many cards you can have in front of a character at once (items, spells, conditions, horror, damage, etc...).

The theme and narrative is applied fairly well.  The game does bring a sense of dread as you add more monsters and progress with worsening characters.  But, the game feels so long that you may find yourself ignoring the flavor text and looking for the skill check requirements.

If you're looking for a Lovecraft game, I think Arkham Horror LCG is easily the best in the genre.

But, MoM... I mean... #20 of all time?!  Has everyone become... dare I say it... INSANE?!  (BOOM!  MYTHOS JOKES!)

It gets a plus for being easy to set up and play and a plus for have reasonable writing and narrative by FFG standards. It gets docked points for only having four scenarios, less interesting minis that the first edition and being rather expensive given the two preceding points. The main market here is people who would rather have an app be their GM than a player with an rpg rule book. If you are looking for a board game experience or an rpg with real legs look elsewhere.

That's not very lovecraftian.

RATING: 6.5
Great production, lots of theme.  Best played with the app stuff cast onto a big TV screen and with the great atmospheric background music properly amplified.  I pity those playing it at a gaming club on a laptop barely visible to one player and no audio.

I love the variable spell side-effects (though that's not new here).

This is just too long for me to want to play very often (~4 hours for first play).  
This may replace betrayal house on the hill, if I could get the play-length down.

Some characters won't have much interesting at all that they can do in some situations, if the team hasn't stuck together and that player is too distant from the action to arrive within range for a round or two.

The app ought to have guided barricade-resolution in with its monster movement resolution in the mythos phase.

The app also ought to have interactive help (click on a word to get a meaning for it).

It'd be nice if the minis fitted and stayed in their stands.

Box is stupidly big.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I’ve been banging the drum for years that HPL games should be fully cooperative so the theme can be fully appreciated by all. That is, there’s not much chance of a happy ending; prepare thyself to make the ultimate sacrifice. Once you introduce egos via a competitive situation, people care more about winning than having a Lovecraft experience and the richness of the genre is lost.  

The first edition never really got off the ground for me and my group but I kept the game around because I saw the possibilities there. To that end, I abandoned MoM 1st edition as competitive and used a fan-made cooperative variant for solo and coop play. It worked well enough to keep the game around.

But then this comes along and takes the game to a new level. I’m writing this comment after playing all the scenarios available to me as an owner of the 1st edition and Forbidden Alchemy expansion. Each one has gotten off the ground in terms of story and tension. The app (with bugs resolved) works great as an AI and mood creator. My tastes are such that I can enjoy each scenario at least twice; once for the discovery and then again to do better. 

Things I particularly like: Setup is a cinch. They streamlined the Horror Checks and the app tracks all checks/attacks. Each story feels different (particularly the mystery that is “Rising Tide”). The potential for new and different stories is vast.

Things I don’t like: The bases of the monsters are not required and cover up the board (I’m going to glue my monsters onto clear bases). I find the traitor mechanic of the Insanity Condition outside my tastes and not viable when controlling two characters apiece. I don’t like that they sell the FA and CotW conversion kits in one $40 box. Those of us who have one or the other have to overpay.

All in all, our plays of this have all been epic in one sense or another with many shouts and groans. At this point all I want is more everything. Really impressed with what they've done with this game especially considering the miss that the first edition was.

Rules notes:
-- A pushed investigator is not moving voluntarily and so would not need to make the evade check. As you note, the investigator doing the pushing would need to evade to perform the push action and evade again should they choose to follow the pushed investigator and leave the space with the monster.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Mansions of Madness has been updated to include an interactive app feature.

The good: 
The rules were set up to get the players up and playing quickly. The tiles had good visuals and were solid. The game promoted a lot of discussion among the players. Unlike Eldritch Horror, the players have to pay attention to the narrative from the app and investigate to discover what their objective is. We got our butts kicked by the game the first round through, so the game wasn't set up to be too simple.

The not so good: 
If the company ever stops supporting the app, then the game could be bricked. The app replaces all of the action cards that describe what happens to a player. Also, you really need four characters on the board to accomplish all the tasks that are required before everybody dies. Movement is so constricted that getting to other characters or to the site where monsters have appeared is frustrating without adequate characters.

After four plays of this game, it's jumped to my favorites list. I really like how quickly new players can start controlling their turn using the app (negating the need for a game master to continually babysit players' actions). I am curious how much repeat playability there will be with completed scenarios. It seems like the clues weren't necessarily changing play-to-play. But as there are already new scenarios for purchase on Steam, I'm not too worried.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Great game if you're looking for a good story set in Lovecraftian universe. Very easy to pick up, you can essentially start playing right away and explain the game as new areas are revealed.

The puzzles are mixed bag. The sliding puzzle is pretty obvious and easy to figure out, then it's just a matter of going through the motions. The image puzzle a bit less so but still predictable. The code puzzle is the most interesting. I would have liked if they were all on the same level of challenge, which should have been easy to accomplish considering how many puzzle games have already been invented before ;)

The biggest problem I have with the game is it's rather simplistic approach on creating a challenge. The game just throws horror and damage at you until you die, forcing you to restart and optimize from past plays (i.e. run through the mansion again collecting critical items as fast as possible to complete the scenario and ignoring search tokens yielding clues or common items) First plays in which you discover new areas are great, subsequent feel too much like a grind.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The production value is really good, nice art work, miniatures and app. It all creates a coherent and immersive world. The story elements were also quite ok, even though the story is super predictable in the end when a huge monster with tentacles appear (who could have guessed that!?). 

So why is the game mechanisms so bad? 
1. You are doing a mission with a strict time limit that you do not know. And when you get to know it you have no chance of still winning. 
2. Search everywhere to find everything, no way to know where to search or go, you just need to do everything. 
3. Random events that could easily kill you for no reason at all. 
4. There is no real strategy in the game.
5. The rules are way too complex for how simple the game is. We spent a good part of the game looking up rules explanations for thing that happen. 

I had a good experience with this game. But it is far from a good game, in fact the best part of it is master mind. So I would rather play master mind than play it again.

RATING: 7.5

RATING: 5
Big disappointment - MoM first edition was innovative, compelling, though with flaws. MoM 2nd edition removes the flaws, but also removes nearly all decision-making and gameplay, kind of like what happens to food that becomes fat-free, sugar-free, and gluten-free in the 'wrong' way - yuck. If you really like the tactical scale of MoM, I recommend Gloomhaven (it's not Cthulhu-themed, but it's extremely good - best fantasy dungeon crawl to date).

RATING: N/A
I will not rate this edition since I do not own it (I own and enjoy the 1st edition). However, a note to anyone considering this edition: MoM 2nd edition cannot be played without the digital companion app. While an app offers some nice advantages, like the ability for all players to play cooperatively and easy content delivery, it also means that your ability to play this game is at the mercy of your device/OS maker's upgrade cycle and FFG's commitment and ability to maintain the app long-term. 

It's not that I don't trust FFG to want to do the right thing. I own and enjoy many FFG titles, and I know that they have the best intentions of making and supporting a great game. But the business incentives simply don't exist for any publisher to ensure that games with apps are well-supported in the future. 

FFG is a business that must sustain itself, and like any business, it must ultimately make decisions based on self-interest. Because it is expensive to develop and maintain software for multiple platforms across multiple upgrade cycles, there is the need for an ongoing revenue stream to support an app-ified game. This fact means an app like this one will only be well-supported while the game and new content delivered via the app are selling well.

Once those revenue streams slow down, the incentive to maintain the app diminishes, and it becomes a distraction and opportunity cost for the publisher. This means there is a fair chance that your app-ified board game will be unsupported when a new device/OS upgrade is released, or when the publisher has a bad quarter.

Put another way, given a choice between maintaining an app for an older game that generates little or no ongoing profit, or putting money into developing the new hotness, which choice do you think any self-interested publisher would make?

As a recent example, take the debacle that was Golem Arcana, a highly touted, app-based game that was discontinued less than 2 years after its debut, and after selling expensive expansion after expansion in the meantime, which should have subsidized app maintenance.

Now, given its scenario-based design, MoM is probably one of the few games where selling additional content on an ongoing basis via an app makes sense, but I for one do not want publishers to focus on producing a slew of low-grade content in lieu of great games. Nor do I want them to design games that are highly content-oriented (usually correlated with mediocre game design).

I'm not saying don't buy this game--I'm sure it's a great game and it looks to have some really cool elements that only an app could provide. But IMHO, it is better for players and the industry for board games to be published according to a discrete, project-based model, where ongoing support and investment are not required. I urge you to know what you're getting into, and second, consider if you want to support this general practice by publishers before voting with your hard-earned money.

Finally, for what it's worth, I do believe that this is one salvo in a grand experiment by FFG/Asmodee to determine if it can dissuade gamers from thinking of board games as durable, infinitely replayable goods, and instead convince them that they are disposable, ephemeral experiences worth paying for again and again. Whatever choice you make, it will pave the way for more of the same in the future.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The best co-op board game I have found yet. There are a lot of great mechanics in this game that are already covered by other reviews, but what needs to be highlighted is the element of surprise/randomness that will keep the replayability high. For instance, your character can gain conditions that have lasting effects through out the game that can affect which actions you are allowed to perform or even altering the way you win.. but you aren't allowed to tell your other players that! How cool is that? There are also 8 unique investigators for you try and the app randomizes the placement of the very detailed and well constructed tiles each time you play. No scenario is ever exactly the same. This combined with the exploration, puzzles, combat and detective work makes it feel like multiple games wrapped into one. And on top of all that the app does a great job of keeping everything tracked and organized for you, including theme music and sound effects. 

The only reason I don't give this a 10 is that I was a bit let down by the miniatures. I understand this was a test run so they most likely just re purposed older methods from the original, but as someone who owns both Descent and Imperial Assault these are a big step down. They are flimsy and small, the investigators lack detail, and the bases for the monsters are a game in itself trying to get them to fit and stay. Pro tip: Some of the bases have different sized holes for the proper figures, figuring this out helps but some still have trouble fitting/staying in their proper base. On top of that, you slide the monster card into the base and then have to flip the base over (with the figure sometimes falling out) to see the flavor text/special abilities. They really should have had the ability on the front with the description on the bottom for reference. But that being said, they are still above the quality we see in a lot of other games, just with FFG the bar is set high so I think they missed the mark just a bit. 

The other side is the price, I paid a similar price for IA and felt that I got more out of it, including the skirmish side of the game. The campaign is longer in terms of hours of play, has a great replayability and I found the flavor text/writing to be superior. But it is a bit like comparing apples and oranges (Imperial player versus co-op with app), however dollar for value IA is better. But content wise it almost feels like they are charging you for the included conversion kit to bring the 1st edition stuff into play.. even if you don't own any 1st edition stuff, so that ticked me off a bit.

BUT if you are looking for a Co-Op game, this is it, look no further you have found it. Just be prepared to work with the minis a bit and if you can find a sale price, although that will probably have to wait for the second or third print at this point.

RATING: 9
One word: wow.  I wasn't sure what to expect here.  I'm a big fan of the First Edition and, after Runebound and the Descent app, it's safe to say I was a bit concerned.  Luckily it turned out just as good as it was pitched.  At it's best, the cooperative aspect is stronger than I'd expected and the app worked wonderfully.  It can't replace the First Edition entirely, but that really is okay.  For those concerned, you can still have a Keeper player.  Give one player the tablet and they can do the narration, handling the interface, and board setup.  Unexpected, but worked rather well.  Oh, and a bonus, the Conversion Kit comes with the base set which is a nice touch, and it's easy to integrate your First Edition components (tiles, characters and monsters).

Honestly, the strongest part is the one with the most contention: the app.  I experienced bugs that have since been patched, nonetheless a problem with relying on an app, but otherwise this really is the centerpiece of the experience.  Since it takes care of most of the setup and running of the game, the players really only need to know a few rules to play which made our first game quick to get into despite needing to reference the rules as new situations arose.  That meant when we picked up a new player it just as easy for them to jump in, a nice touch.  Unfortunately this seems to mean the manual is a bit lighter as a result.  The book doesn't seem to address what to do when multiple characters, for example, are all valid targets for a Mythos effect or monster attack.

While it's the centerpiece, that means it's also needs to work perfectly.  When it crashes or freezes there is no way to get back to your game and yes, we experienced this twice.  It is utterly frustrating.  The app is also a gate keeper to additional content and there is no way to set up your own scenarios.  This may not bug everyone but it's definitely worth noting since it also means you can't add content at all, even official, unless the app specifically allows for it ( ie: multiple base sets or expansions ).  Not to mention even if the random missions work well, you'll want new stories and you can't do them yourself.  That means Fantasy Flight will either go the DLC route, which I have mixed feelings towards, or they'll do smaller expansions which coincide with updates to the app, which I have mixed feelings on, or a combination of the two.  The potential is there to add updates which add new puzzles and scenarios but since we don't know how that will be used, I remain cautiously optimistic.  Here's hoping we get some sort of scenario editor as well.

Overall though, my concerns do not get in the way of the game.  It's tons of fun to play, comes highly recommended and I really hope the Second Edition gets plenty of love.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Awesome, one of my favourite games now with a less time consuming preparation time and playable cooperatively. It has mid quality miniatures and only 4(+2 if you have previous edition components) playable scenarios so expansions will definitely be needed.

RATING: 3
Another app-boardgame hybrid from FFG to get the most money from both marketgroups, keep printing costs down and adoring itself towards buzzword-loving investors.

+ I guess it made some rich guy a little richer and provided job experience for some people. 

+ It has some nice artwork, regurgitated from a bunch of other games. 

+ It has a really big, empty box that your cat will love! 

+ I suppose having ugly, wobbly miniatures instead of markers, a dull voice over and an uninspired syntheziser score on repeat, might help some people who otherwise lack imagination to feel more invested in the game. 

- For us, however, it didn't add anything but a hefty price tag and mild amusement for novelty's sake. We'd rather read the text out loud and provide our own soundtrack. Unfortunately, we still have to pay for the crap that FFG force upon us.

- The app is awful. On a high end pad it still crashes often and has a ton of graphic and gameplay bugs. 

- The app sucks battery power more than a FFG executive can leech itself to a lucrative IP.  

- Despite marketed as a dungeon explorer/monster basher, the app forces you to play in-app puzzle games whether you find those tedious or not. 

- Takes forever to play. 

- Gives you the task to fiddle around with cards, when those could have been handled by the app. When that app so clearly wants to be a computer game, it feels kind of sad to not let it fulfill its dreams. 

- Random set ups that doesn't work well with scripted events. Either this is an amateurish mistake or a cynical attempt to provide 'replay value'. 

- Suffers from classical FFG game design in that it rewards min-maxing your resource managment and character selection. 

- Inspired by the modern age of customer responsibility dodging, the rule book refers any technical rules problems to the app, which of course doesn't respond to your rules question in other ways than crashing. 

- The only African-American person in the game is white. 

- Despite all the clues and exploration options, your best bet to win the game is to surge forward to the last room tile asap, killing or avoiding monsters in your way.

RATING: 8.75
I love this game!! I want more of it anyway I can get it!! But therein lies the problem. Absolutely love the integration with the app that really allows you to dive deeply into the story, gives a whole new experience from your standard board game of this style (even more so then other FF games), and gives you really good immersion into the mythos itself. However I'm the type of board game player that will certainly play each and every storyline available on the app probably even multiple times. It is very wonderful that they change monsters, floor plans, and even minor story elements to make replay different every time, but really sad they did not include the traditional Overlord option of 1st edition or other Fantasy Flight games like Descent. It takes away the ability to run it like a competitive style game which can be lots of fun too!! Of course you can still create your own stories and run it as you would a full fledged role-playing game (assigning monsters, creating plotline,even adding traps), but again no information was really provided for this leaving a lot to trial and error and improvisation to keep it fun yet still fair. Having access to things like Overlord Cards* from FF's Descent just allowed the person "running" the game to be more engaged during game play by throwing in small advantages and environment changes by reacting to player choices. I know it's probably just a personal preference, but just seems like they already had the framework there from 1st edition. For the cost of the game (MSRP $99.95) I would think an extra booklet and a handful of cards would be well within reason and more than appreciated.

RATING: 7
The second edition is an improvement, using a computer aid to handle the gamesmaster role so all the players can be fully coop.  It also speeds up play by keeping track of a lot of the situation, presenting puzzles, and providing atmosphere.  The scenarios and the game components are high quality.  It provides an RPG experience without a GM and so is nice from that standpoint.  On the other hand, without a GM the role-playing aspect is quite absent.  In general, I'd much rather play Call of Cthulhu with a GM to get the full RPG experience.  It's also a less satisfying boardgame experience than Arkham Horror or Edritch Horror since the scope of strategic decisions and powerups is much more limited.  So as a hybrid between RPG and boardgame I find it less satisfying then either.  That said, if someone wants to play it, I'm willing.  It's just not my first choice.

RATING: 7.5
2016-10-25

Initial Rating: 7.0 (February 2016)

Cthulhu mythos is one of my favorite themes, so when FFG does a game about it, I just have to buy it :D. I have played and liked Mansions of Madness before and I was expecting more of the same, but the app REALLY catapult it to the next level. I’m very impressed by it!

Mansions of Madness: Second Edition is an easy game to teach, you can do it under 10 minutes. Playtime is long, perhaps too long for what it offers. Due to this, I think it is best with 3 players, with more the downtime begins to be too much.

The decisions continue to be easy and (for the most part) obvious, but as Mansions of Madness: Second Edition is a thematic game you don’t mind much.

I´m a bit saddened that no player can be the Keeper now, that´s always my role in the games and I enjoy it very much. Still the app can be a better Keeper (due to all the procedures needed) and it is neat to solve puzzles on it, besides it solves the issue that if the Keeper made a mistake during the setup, the game was broken. It is also very cool that you don’t setup all the map at the beginning, the app lets you know which tiles goes where (just like the first Descent used to do). I really hope they translate the app soon to Spanish as many friends aren’t comfortable with English. 

I love that the game comes with different scenarios, but it comes with one less than the previous edition, however, with the app I think (but I’m not sure) that every play is very different. I LOVE that you can use the first edition materials in it and the game already comes with an upgrade ready for it :thumbs up:.

The production values are very high as is usual from FFG, some of my miniatures came with the base damaged though. Inserting the square monster tokens into the bases is hard because they are very tight, if you make a mistake here undoing it is hard, besides the tokens aren’t used in the game as the app supplied all the information.

The game is indeed expensive game but for what comes in the box (it is full of cards, cardboard and miniatures) it is worth it.

I never play solo games, but as the app does all the upkeep, perhaps I will give it a shot.

[o]
•	I love that the big baddies (Star Spawns?) do make an appearance in the very first scenario :D.
•	I’m ambivalent by the insanity goals. When a player goes insane he receives a new goal and he can win alone, become a traitor or lose alone even if he helped the group all along. I’m not sure how I feel about this, but it is definitely thematic (perhaps I’m biased as in my game I was the only player who lost due to this :D). I need more plays to verify.
[/o]

Bottom line, Mansions of Madness: Second Edition is an impressive thematic game. My desire to play it again is very high. The app works flawlessly and I hope it fixes the very low replay value of the previous incarnation of the game (FFG can add more scenarios just updating the app). I think I will buy all its expansions. 

Current Rating: 7.5

RATING: 10
This game is fantastic. It takes all the things I love from games like Betrayal at House on the Hill and everything I love in games like Elder Sign and Eldritch Horror. You explore the mansion through one of the scenario's to uncover random events and ultimately solve the case. The way the mansion is setup is also random every time you play. What makes this game so amazing and so immersive though is the use of the app on an IPad. The app randomizes the events, the mansion, and even the items that you will encounter throughout the scenario. The game begins with the app reading aloud an intro to the players and then going straight into the game while playing ambient background music. Now I'm not going to explain how the entire game is played, however, it is simple but extremely fun and immersive while you search for clues to complete your end game goal.  Plus the best part is that the app does all the book keeping for you while letting you roll your own dice. The app/ game integration is amazing. I highly recommend this one!

Miniatures aside, this is a top shelf, dark and engaging cooperative game filled with narrative, choices and horror. From a graphic design standpoint there are few board games that look better. Mansions of Madness 2nd Edition's mechanics are straightforward and players rarely will get bogged down in rules and confusion which has hampered other games in the Lovecraft universe. If only the mini's and their sloppy bases fit together properly this would be a benchmark game. Still, anyone seeking to explore the depths of madness with (or without) companion should check this fantastic game out.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
10-24-2016 - Ok, 2 plays in and it's settled.  This is the best game in the freaking world.  It's exactly what I want in my ideal game.  Well, almost...

The good:  Exploration that feels meaningful, upgrading your character, asymmetrical powers, cooperative goals, totally immersive, twists and turns that completely change the game, not too easy and not too hard, FUN, I want to play this thing over and over.

(I just finished these comments, reading back over them now, and I realize I never mentioned the app!  So more "the good:"  The app is great, it works really well, adds to the experience and lessens in-game complication, though it seriously - SERIOUSLY - needs a Back button!)

The bad:  I'm worried about replay value after getting through all the scenarios (but we're pretty far from that right now) and the horror checks at every Mythos can get a little tedious IMHO.  The biggest "negative" for me that I really don't care that much about: although I can customize my character with equipment (AWESOME), I can only do it with equipment I FIND.  If I don't FIND a cool weapon, I don't GET a cool weapon.  I mean, there will be weapons around, and someone will find one, and trading is pretty open if you're in the same spot with another investigator, but I like games where I save up some currency and buy a super cool weapon.  You're at the mercy of what's included in the scenario in MOM2, and it makes perfect sense given the theme and what's going on during a game (you just show up to a crazy building and play the whole thing out with what you brought in and what you can find), but when evaluating the game as a whole against "my most perfectest game int he world ever", that's a bit of a drawback.  But not enough, I'm still giving this monster a 10.

One last thing that bugs me is that, having invested hundreds of dollars in the pre-painted Arkham minis, MOM2 introduces new monsters and new characters.  My collection is no longer complete!  Well, for Arkham it is, at least as complete as it's going to get, but I want pre-painted minis for these new guys!  Sure, I could just learn to paint and paint the in-the-box minis myself, but I'm entirely too lazy.

Still, 10 for sure.  I seriously la-la-love this game.  Don't mean maybe.

Mansions of Madness: Second Edition is a fully cooperative, app-driven board game of horror and mystery for one to five players that takes place in the same universe as Eldritch Horror and Elder Sign. Let the immersive app guide you through the veiled streets of Innsmouth and the haunted corridors of Arkham's cursed mansions as you search for answers and respite. Eight brave investigators stand ready to confront four scenarios of fear and mystery, collecting weapons, tools, and information, solving complex puzzles, and fighting monsters, insanity, and death. Open the door and step inside these hair-raising Mansions of Madness: Second Edition. It will take more than just survival to conquer the evils terrorizing this town.

RATING: 7
I haven't played the original, so I can't compare to it. This is the first app-assisted game I've played, and my feelings are mixed. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Initial impressions: Seems so far to have good immersion but not strategically based at all. By that I mean the exploration of the map (which I assume will be a little bit different from game to game even with the same scenario) is fun and you get a good  feel of how a haunted house actually would be like. However, the lack of knowledge of the ramifications of your actions means there isn't much in the way of smart vs. dumb decisions in the game so far.

The app really helps throw a lot of things under the hood and makes this probably accessible to those who can play medium-light games, which is a nice bonus. It also made jumping into your first game a lot easier.

As someone who admittedly doesn't have a strong preference of minis vs. standees with good artwork, I wish they had not had the not very special minis and just had used standees cut out in the shape of the monsters and stats on the side. This is for several reasons: the art on the monster tokens is very good and the minis' bases covers that up, this would have required less first game set up, this would have significantly reduced the cost of the game, the minis since several have similar poses and the sculpts are not excellent quality take a little more than a glance to confirm which one is which, and finally some of the minis won't even go into their bases properly.

Will need to impress me more if I am to consider getting my money's worth (granted, this is the most expensive game I have ever bought), hopefully on the strategic decisions side and having a lot of replayability.

EDIT 1: I know there is strategy in the puzzles, but I meant more long term strategy of e.g. exploring vs. searching, board positioning, etc. I'm seeing some benefits of e.g. staying far apart to limit horror effects vs. being close together to gang up on monsters, but it still feels like more of a pick your poison than you should optimize for this based on if e.g you are more worried about damage vs. horror. Also some item management involved.

I played the 1st scenario again and while I was initially quite excited that the initial map was different, I was disappointed that the scenario played out rather similarly. I think this might end up having the same problem Castle Ravenloft had for me, where while each play is seemingly different with different rooms, they all feel the same because each play is just of gobbled mess of random tiles rather than a narrative (yes is the overaching narrative that you need to beat the game, but the procedures to get there was basically the same).

EDIT 2: Forgot to mention that the flavor text gives you some clues of what actions does what, which is nice; kind of TIME Stories-esque in giving some basis of taking which actions. In fact come to think of it this game feels a lot like TIME Stories to me: besides the clues in the flavor text there is a strong exploration feel, the skill checks testing is kind of mediocre, and you gradually unravel mystery with future game attempts allowing you to ignore certain things that you know probably won't help you. Unfortunately as of right now the smaller amount of deduction makes it not as enjoyable as the Asylum TIME Stories scenario (though better bang for your buck especially since the scenarios still are somewhat replayable, even without the somewhat variable setup). Some people might like this game and not TIME Stories though based on the not as clunky combat and skill checks, more focus on the monsters (even if combat isn't the main focus of this game), there is less of a repetitive feel from playing a scenario over, the aforementioned replayability, you often lose by dying rather than just not being efficient enough with your time, and the app making it feel more like there is a sentient house out to get you rather than static cards.

It might be that each scenario has a different setup based on the heroes chosen, as playing similar heroes got me a similar setup (or might be this just randomly happened). In this sense I might have to take back what I said of my comparison to Castle Ravenloft, and it might actually have the opposite problem that Mice and Mystics also had: once you've played a scenario once there's not a huge reason to play it again however so far played 1st scenario 3 times, 2nd one once, and maybe the later scenarios feel more different game to game.

Despite having roughly the same number of monsters as Mice and Mystics however (which had too few and you were fighting the same mobs basically every other tile), the less focus on monster fighting in this game makes that less of a problem, and the app controlling them makes them perform a little bit differently each round.

Speaking more about the app, the ambient music is nice, along with the occasional narration. The app apparently saves results of when you fail things so that if you barely fail some checks, it makes others easier for you which I also like. Controlling the mythos phase and what flavor text is for what token as I mentioned before makes this game a lot more accessible. I do wish they had provided some detail on how it decides what the party encounters and also how the monsters act, so even if you don't know everything you can still somewhat strategize more. For example, knowing if this monster dishes out more horror or damage so to better choose who deals with it, or if it favors causing the hero to perform certain skill checks over another. I think this game works a lot better with a tablet; passing my phone around is not ideal since it is hard for one person to see it at a time.

EDIT 3: Only have played this with 2 or 3 investigators so far, but seems much easier with 3, as while the monster health scales with number of investigators and there may or may not be slightly more monster spawns, there are still overall less monsters per hero allowing for greater survivability, along with the same amount to search while having more actions to do so.

EDIT 4: I've played all the scenarios, and while the first time for each investigation is great, future plays, even of the last scenario which has different people be the evildoers from scenario from scenario and lets you get into different locked areas depending on what order you explore the own, are rather stale. This is because the overall layout is the same, so while there are new things, 80% of it is a rehash, and while you can choose to ask different questions of the people, since you only have a vague idea of the ramifications of your questions, it doesn't feel as fresh as trying a different strategy/tactic in a different game. I don't know, maybe I should wait a couple months and play it again as I had fun replaying Mice and Mystics again after a little while, but that a lot of the optimization of this game is tied to the hidden knowledge which is no longer a surprise makes me skeptical and I'm kind of tempted to put it on my for trade list already.

RATING: 8
Good: 
Engaging story and theme. The app does a good job of making the board feel like a creepy and slightly off world the players are exploring.  

App helps make exploration engaging and haunted, all the while allowing for quick setup. 

Good selection of characters. Even with just the base game we find someone we would like to play.

For the most part the components are top notch. 

Bad: 
Depending on who you play with the game can be paced on the slow side and may get a little repetitive. 

For most scenarios you only have two actions and are forced to keep moving toward the objective or fail. While this is suppose to prevent players from taking forever to complete the objective the timer feels arbitrary and a little punishing at times. 

Components is a little mixed. The plastic figures and stands are a little on the flimsy side compared to other games.

RATING: 8

Pros:  1) Engaging gameplay (most important aspect of a game!).  2) Challenging but well balanced.  3) Fun storylines that keep me interested.  4) Lots of paths to take and decisions make each play different.  5) Learning the game wasn't that difficult.  6) And most importantly...it's FUN!

Cons:  1) the models ( low quality compared to similarly priced games and don't fit the bases very well at all). and 2) base game only comes with 4 scenarios.  Yes I know each one plays differently each time, but I think they should have more DLC or scenarios right out of the box.  4 is pretty low and for a $100 game I expect more.  

But despite those minor gripes I think this game is excellent!
