RATING: 6.5
05/02/2018 1 partida a 5 jugadores
50% videojuego y 50% juego de mesa.

Entiendo el porqué gustan tanto estos juegos. Te cuentan una pequeña historia, el despliegue del juego es muy vistoso y si encima la temática te gusta pues es normal que estos juegos se valoren tan bien. Pero yo busco algo distinto en los juegos que no encuentro en estos. Me gusta que me cuenten una historia pero para eso prefiero un libro o una película. Que un juego sea bonito y espectacular es un punto a favor, pero no lo más importante para mí. Y la temática Lovecraft, aun gustándome, me deja un poco indiferente porque Lovecraft, como escritor, me parece un poco flojo. Yo lo que más valoro en un juego son sus mecánicas, y en MoM son muy simples. Básicamente vas haciendo lo que dice la aplicación y nunca una decisión compleja. Vas moviendo tu personaje explorando la mansión y combatiendo monstruos, pero todo con mecánicas simples y haciendo lo que diga la rubia, esto es, la aplicación. Encima el juego es bastante largo.

En definitiva, no me importa volver a jugarlo y además lo disfrutaré porque aburrirme no me aburrió. El entreturno se hace entretenido porque vas viendo lo que hacen los demás investigadores y los enemigos. Pero hay otros muchos juegos que me gustan más y con los que disfruto más.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game has become one of my favorite games, especially when looking at games I can play solo.  Story elements are very immersive.  It's very easy to get caught up in the narrative the app plays out.  Puzzles in the app are challenging enough to fit a wide audience, rules are easy to teach (taught the game in about 5-10 minutes with my friends.)  

Can't rate a 10 for 2 reasons. First, miniature quality is subpar for an FFG game.  If I open the Jabba's Palace expansion for Imperial Assault (released around the same time), the models have a lot more detail and are much better molded.  Positioning of models is questionable at best.  The bases for the models are abhorrent, and I will be replacing them.  I don't know what the developers were thinking with the ugly, black bases.  If you aren't replacing bases, I would play with just the monster tokens, which is disappointing to say about a $100 MSRP game.

Second reason is I wish the app did more.  It tracks almost everything, but leaves monster movement and line of sight to the players.  Would be easier to play if the app tracked that, too.  More scenarios would also be fantastic.

A purchase of MoM 2nd Ed will not leave you wanting.  If you see it somewhere, pick it up.

RATING: 9

I'd rate this higher except for the long play time and fiddly nature of the game.  These aren't knocks, as they are part of the genre and help make it thematic and engrossing, but at the same time, this isn't a game you can just bring out anytime.  There may also be issues with replay value.  I will need to revise my rating after a few more plays.

RATING: 8
I really liked the first edition.  The Mansion allowed for a great setting and led to some of the most memorable times I've had gaming in the past few years.  However, it was horrible to set up and that drastically limited the enjoyment I got out of it.

The Second Edition gets rid of this problem almost entirely.  From box to getting the game going is on the order of 10 minutes instead of an hour.  Additionally, the app makes the co-op aspect much stronger and makes a 2-player game possible (if somewhat complicated).

The only issues that I have with the game are that it tends to drag on a little bit longer than it should.  If new missions come out that are SHORTER or just consistently exciting then this could be a real winner.

RATING: 8
Espectacular! FFG nos da una lección de como las tabletas pueden aportar buen valor a los juegos sin que este pierda el factor humano que tanto aporta a los juegos de mesa. 
MofM puede llegar a ser un juego temático perfecto, todo está inmerso en el universo de Lovecraft. Lo he podido jugar varias veces y es superior a la 1era versión, pero igual siento que vuelve a caer en los mismos errores de los otros juegos de esta temática (a excepción de Arkham Horror The Card Game), el nivel de complejidad de las reglas le quita el atmósfera de la premura y el terror. 
Es un juego espectacular para jugadores experimentados, para nuevos puede ser pesado (claramente menos que la 1era versión :) ). 
Pero porque no un 9 o un 10? Porque nuevamente las reglas, las fases, los eventos, y ese tipo de consideraciones restan a la atmósfera, mientras que en otros juegos (Pandemic Reign of Cthulhu) la mecánica va ayudando a ir sintiendo la tensión, en este juego los sucesos o eventos en algún momento carecen de sentido, simplemente sucede porque tenían que suceder en el juego. 
Fuera de eso! Espectacular! :D

RATING: 8
I was pretty skeptical of this at first. There are some really lousy miniatures in the box, and the storytelling is a bit hokey in places, but this has become one of my wife and I's favorite games to play together. My rating has been dropping mainly because I'm getting a little burned out on keeping up with the expansions and DLC, especially since Fantasy Flight is charging for every little thing. I find that Mythos Tales covers a lot of the same ground in a more approachable way. And the dice rolling, UGH, the dice rolling. It bothered me at first, then it didn't, and now it makes me question why they didn't make this a full blown app since all you're really doing yourself is rolling dice and moving your character around.

I'm still having fun with this, but for a game that once had real contention of knocking off my favorite game (Arkham Horror), it's not having as much staying power as I once hoped. If nothing else, I figure I can use the minis in my copy of Arkham Horror, so no real loss there.

RATING: 3
There are almost no interesting decisions and replayability is non-existent once you've played a scenario, so it's just this huge expensive box that sits on my shelf collecting dust. The stories are really basic (get into a house, kill a cultist, leave the house, that kind of stuff) with lame-ass puzzles thrown in at random intervals and cheesy graphics and sound effects. Lovecraft here is just a pretext, which is fine by me since I'm not particularly a fan, but at the very least his writings could have been put to use to flesh out whatever semblance of plot there is in the scenarios presented here.

Also this is not much of a boardgame, much more like an app that's just one tweak away from being fully self-sufficient (you would just have to be able to move the monsters on the app's map). If you like staring at a screen for several hours you might as well play a video game but that's just my opinion.

I have only played solo so it's quite possible that lots of people who enjoy this enjoy the conviviality just as much as the game itself, which quite frankly is not much of a game, more of a choose your own adventure with even less agency on the part of the players. Ask yourself if you have friends who can really contribute to the mood and get past all the cheesiness and limited gameplay before buying.

RATING: 3
Have to say, I totally don't get the raving reviews about this edition / game.  Someone gave this a "1" with a comment that I actually completely agree with.

I was very excited to play it, and even had it as a "must buy" on my wishlist going into Gencon.  However, after actually playing a demo and then playing the game, in my opinion this is not a board game.  It is a very passive experience in which there is very little decision making, and in which the physical board and bits simply don't matter.  Don't get me wrong, the app is cool.  It is just that the app is so nice that it made me frustrated with the board game... I'd rather play the thing fully on the computer.  Why roll dice anymore?  Why have figures on a board?  Why have cards in front of you?  Given that the app is already telling you to do this and that and that, I'd rather simply have this be a multi-person online game.

Now, as someone who appreciates story-telling board games, I do feel obligated to explain why this one provides a "passive" experience while other provide a more active experience.  T.I.M.E. Stories, for example, is "passive" in the sense that the entire game involves uncovering a story.  And yet, I found that game to be a really fun, interesting experience.  Partially, that has to do with the dynamic in which players have information that they then need to relay to the rest of the group.  In that sense they become active conduits of information.  Also, use of the card tableau provides a scene that oddly encourages players to actively fill in the gaps.  Mansions of Madness, on the other hand, provides paragraphs of text that merely get read out loud.  You might pass the app around and have different people read it, but it comes down to passive recitation.  In addition, I found that over time, it becomes more like, "Yeah, yeah, just tell me what I need to roll."  You roll, and then the next person recites their paragraph.  They roll, and then the next person recites.  ....

I've basically played every one of Fantasy Flight's Lovecraft titles, and I found this one to be the absolute worst.  Very, very boring.  It's like reading a book, but passing the book around from one person to the next with each reading a paragraph.  Not an unpleasant thing to do around a campfire...but also not a board game.

RATING: 4
These RPG-ish games really aren't for me, but I was suckered in by Mansions Of Madness' theme and polish.

MoM does try to get Lovecraft right. Losing a 3 hour game to an otherworldly horror where everyone winds up either crazy or dead, having impotently tried to beat back the monsters and failed, is just how Lovecraft would write it.

And fail you will. Should you replay the scenarios in attempt to win, you'll discover that events repeat exactly, the only change being the randomization of items, reducing replay value to zero. Also, MoM scales for number of players poorly, fluctuating monster health and little else. As most scenarios involve searching for items, attempting with only a few players is very difficult.

The detailed, interlinking map tiles have good, albeit sinister artwork and provide good immersion. The miniatures are well-modeled, but mount poorly on their ridiculously over-sized bases. The other components are your standard Fantasy Flight fare: Many decks of tiny cards, dice, and lots of chits.

MoM:2E suffers from all the issues I have with similar titles, like roll-for-success mechanics, clunky rules, long game set-up and play time, plus it now requires a tablet to play.

MoM:2E's reliance on an app to run the game does allow for all players to actually play, rather than force one to be Keeper, but this spreading hybridization of games with computers is an issue to question. For one, how long will the app be supported? Like VHS games of yesteryear, the technology may one day no longer exist for this edition to even run. Also, as helpful as it is in running the behind the scenes stuff on a detail-heavy game like this, the game could have been entirely done on the app and it feels like they just removed a few features to sell it as a 'board game' instead.

RATING: 9
Un très gros jeu que ces Demeures de l’épouvante version 2. La nouveauté étant bien évidemment l'implémentation d'une application qui pilote le jeu sous pc/smartphone/tablette.
C'est une très grosse claque que l'on prend direct. Que ce soit en solo ou à plusieurs c'est un vrai régal. Les musiques, les sons, la narration dans l'application sont très bien rendues, une réelle ambiance/atmosphère est palpable pendant le jeu. Le combat ne sont pas répétitif et originaux, notamment par le fait de subir des dégats mentaux qui peuvent rendre fou sans perdre la partie sur le moment.
L'objectif commun des investigateurs (joueurs) est inconnu lorsque l'on démarre un scénario et l'on va pouvoir le découvrir en cours de partir. Il sera annoncé par l'application. Cependant, dès lors où un investigateur aura perdu sa santé mentale, il recevra secrètement une carte folie qui prendra le pas sur son objectif, rendant ainsi les autres investigateurs suspicieux à son égard. La partie s'en retrouve totalement changée.
Les events générées par l'application sont aussi très sympa pour être dans l'ambiance.
L'univers de Cthulhu est superbement retranscrit.
Tout c'est c'est le bon côté... Pour les mauvais je commencerai déjà par évoquer la boite du jeu : il y a plein de figurines qui s'emboitent dans des socles (pas toujours correctement) et aucun rangement n'est prévu dans la boîte! Bémol : les extensions impactent tous les scénarios avec les monstres générés durant la partie
Il y a aussi pas mal de matériel mais à déployer au fur et à mesure de l'avancement de la partie donc ca va mais le jeu n'est tout de même pas simple à sortir quand on part du principe que le scénario d'initiation nous a pris 3h de jeu alors qu'il est annoncé comme étant le plus court par l'application.

RATING: 9
The rules are really simple and streamlined. It makes playing this game a pleasure - easy to pick up, easy to explain etc. All of the failings of the first edition are fixed.

The app is excellent. Really no problem to use at all.

Thematically the game is first rate. Artwork, sounds, gameplay etc. all add to a fine experience. Set up times are great too.

As a solo game, this is probably the best thing in my collection. 

One failing of this game, and is was a problem with version is the minis. Firstly, although some of them are pretty nice to look at, none of them fit on the bases properly, even with glue. Secondly, all of  the info and stats for each creature is in the app, so why have the cardboard chits too? Then, the bases of the creatures are thicker than those of the characters so they look a bit crap together.

My solution is - paint up and use the characters and use the chits instead of the minis. I might paint them later, but found that I am not even using the ones I kept from first ed.

One final thing - although there is quite a bit of playing in the scenarios in the core box, 4 full scenarios isn't very generous ( I paid 100 euro for this game and am only using 8 of the minis). I will pay for more in the future and gladly so, but I would expect and be grateful for free new adventures in the future.

Overall though, this is an awesome game. Now I want apps like this for Descent and Imperial Assault.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Mansions of Madness: Second Edition is a scenario-based dungeon-crawler based in H. P. Lovecraft's Cthulhu mythos.  While there is some combat, the game puts more of an emphasis on exploration and puzzle-solving.  The game is fully cooperative and moderated by a mobile app, that provides information about board setup and what happens when you interact with various features and monsters.  However, the app doesn't track everything--skill checks are still performed with physical dice, and the app doesn't store information about characters' locations, damage, or items.  This is nice as players can still make informed decisions about dice risk, and you don't have to keep fiddling with the app to keep it abreast of everything that's going on.  However, the app does know some things about what's going on, such as which rooms have been explored and which items have been inspected, and clearly presents challenges based on that--the app obscures why some of the events are happening, which can be a little unusual for a boardgamer.  Personally, I was pleasantly surprised by the gameplay of this game, as I found the mystery to be very intriguing, and interacting with the world to find out what happens definitely kept my interest.

(1 play)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Alright, where to begin with this one. A lot to like in this and a lot to hate. First off, I'll start off with the hate. The minis are pretty terrible, at least their bases and getting them to stay put are just bad. You HAVE to glue them in otherwise they fall the second you move them. That wouldn't be so bad, but the bases are huge and ugly. Not to mention the minis are pretty poor quality, especially the small figures. Lots of flash and cheaply made.

The game is very luck based as almost everything comes down to a die roll. Yes, you have some thought of what you're going to do in the game, but it's mainly check a search token and roll some dice. Only having 2 actions makes it tough to do anything outside of move and search.

You only have 4 (5?) scenarios in the base game and each expansion that is included after only includes 1 scenario. I'd be more than happy to buy some cheap DLC and just use the tiles/monsters that I have in the game already instead of paying $30-50 for a few extra of each. I'm unsure of how replayable each scenario is once you figure out the "trick" behind it.

The game also focuses heavily on the app. Outside of having monsters and figures on the screen, the rest of the game is literally moot. You're so glued to the app, it's hard to focus on the board. I'd suggest keeping it to one person and having them control the app as a kind of DM. They'll still be invested in the game, but much less so than the other players.

The good things about the game are the awesome story aspects and the completely co-op gameplay. Having a DM before sucked as a tiny mistake here could literally make the game unplayable. Having an app control the situation makes it impossible to fuck up, but you also feel like you have less control over the game. The nice thing though is you don't need to fiddle with cards to attack.

I also like that I can use the monster figures in Arkham Horror to add a bit more suspense to the game.

Overall, a mild disappointment as a game, but a fun story romp that's basically an interactive book. I want to keep getting more of it, but I don't know if it's wise to keep investing so much money into it. I'd like to see how they flesh out new stories.

RATING: 4

RATING: 7
Dropping my rating after several plays from a 9 to a 7. This is just hard to get to the table. People don't enjoy the difficulty spike which comes as the game progresses. I think the pacing could be better, and this could be driven by the app fortunately, so it's not a total condemnation or un-fixable. The problem is that the first third of most games is manageable, but as the story unfolds the board suddenly becomes thick with monsters which are incredibly powerful. Only certain characters even stand a chance at fighting so campaigns have a tendency to devolve into a death spiral which is impossible to pull out of. This has led to a lot of "oh it's over, let's just call it" as soon as things start to unravel...and I hate to admit they're usually right. I want to love this game, and I want my friends to love to play it, but as it stands that's become difficult barring some house-rules which might make it more manageable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
An interesting, messy, and very thematic survival horror game. It's a pretty fiddly game, with lots of tokens and cards and map tiles. The app helps with organizing all of it (I shudder to think how incredibly fiddly the game would be without it), but it is also an annoying chokepoint for the gameplay. The app is basically the GM, so each time someone wants to take an action someone has to tap something on the app to see what happens / what dice we need to roll / etc. The person running the app needs to be very on-the-ball or the game will drag out quite a bit.

The app does add some tension over a traditional board game, since it might tell you to make a roll and report the results without knowing what your target value is. That makes the game feel a bit less mechanical and more mysterious. It also does a good job of adding variety without making situations where the game just feels fundamentally broken (like Betrayal at House on the Hill does). However, I'm not sure I like it since it starts to feel like the whole game would be better off as a video game instead.

I like the exploration and the general feeling of playing through a pulpy horror novel. I don't like how long the game takes to play through, even with the app. I also don't really like that the game has players play through actual puzzles on the phone/tablet at various points. This really slows down the game and I'd prefer that in-game puzzles rely on the characters' skills, not the players'. The whole thing just feels a bit messy and in need of some clever streamlining that preserves the good parts of the experience and polishes away the lengthy downtime and complicated rules that you need to constantly reference. I think it's likely I'm just not in the target audience for these kinds of games though. People who love them seem to like that they aren't elegantly designed.

RATING: 9
A very thematic adventure horror game. The app integration is well done and does not take away from the experience at all. Fully cooperative, it has always been a fun time when this makes it to the table. The art fits nicely with the mood and tone of everything else and the execution is good. Expansions are overpriced as well and fall victim to the same issues previously described. But ignoring the pricing issues, this is an excellent game that doesn't disappoint.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I played my first game of this new edition last night. Five player.

This is an improvement on the first edition of Mansions of Madness. The app aspect works well by adding a bit more mystery, reducing clutter and speeding things up. It also has atmospheric sound effects.

I guess it's the best game in its specific niche. It's better and more developed than Betrayal On House On The Hill, which seems like its main competitor in this genre. 

It's possibly more fun than other 'Lovecraft horror' games of different styles (Eldritch Horror, Elder Sign etc). Although Eldritch Horror is perhaps more satisfyingly 'thinky'. The first edition of A Study In Emerald is still (as of 2017) my favourite Lovecraft game by a mile.

It is difficult to score this game, so I won't. Its 'knockabout' nature makes it a very subjective experience. Despite its current score of 8.4 on this site, many 'sophisticated' players will hate it.

Most of the time players make decisions that are either obvious or blind guesswork. You go into a room, clues appear, you roll dice to 'explore' them, sometimes you take treasure or damage and then sometimes you fight. Rarely the (very chaotic) events manage to create genuinely tricky decisions, but then you might as well flip a coin to decide - after all, you'll be rolling plenty more dice to see what happens next.

The fighting element is much less developed than it is in Descent 2 or Imperial Assault, which look similar but have a lot more fighting and less clue-finding than Mansions of Madness. However Mansions of Mansions is much better than those games at giving players interesting combinations of items etc to work with. Mansions of Madness may have more replayability than them (despite the clue-finding aspect) because in Mansions of Madness a lot more random stuff can happen with item cards you draw, injuries you take etc. So every game will generate quirky new situations.

Overall, if you want to watch little 3cm tall miniatures dice-rolling their way through a hostile building full of fun items and nasty horrors, then go for it. You might love it. I'd play it again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Pros
• excellent Lovecraftian theme and lots of fun to play. This is my niece's favorite game.
• app allows everyone to participate, taking over most game management tasks
• app greatly improves map exploration enjoyment and increases the element of surprise
• app design is well done, keeping players' attention on the board; but, of course, it's still an app...

Cons  
• The app. This app works very well but mixing electronic devices with board games puts you in a different head space. You have to figure out how to run the app, and deal with how to make it do what you want it to do. Even though this app is well designed, it still takes you out of the game a little bit. 
• My group does not like the app driven puzzles in the game at all. These take us out of the game and brings play to a stop. This is the biggest negative to the use of the app. When we run into one of these puzzles, we groan and then  do whatever it takes to get past the puzzle – we don't try to follow the rules for solving them, we just click through to the solution to get it out of the way. These puzzles do not feel at all thematic, despite what other reviewers have said, and I wish there was a way to disable the puzzles. When a puzzle comes up, it is time for a break while someone, usually me, deals with the puzzle so the game can continue. I can't stress enough what a bad design decision these electronic app puzzles were.
• Fantasy Flight rulebooks are divided into two booklets, which can be a source of frustration at times. I prefer one well organized rulebook, indexed, with gameplay and component illustrations, and explanations of all iconography. 
• Sometimes the app refers to items and monsters for which there are no corresponding components provided. We have all of the expansions, so that's not the problem. I think there is something that I'm not understanding, so this is probably my fault.
• Long play time; we have not found the listed scenario play time durations to be accurate. However, our group seems to be slow because this is a problem we have with many games. Another group may not have this problem. I've found that the person running the app needs to keep the group focused in order to move the story forward; the app isn't intelligent and can't substitute for a good game master in this regard. 
• After a time, the descriptions provided by the app become repetitive. 
• App detracts from game if device is passed around; best to assign one person to run the app; the app should be thought of as a game master assistant, allowing the game master to participate in the adventure. 
• The game is best when a scenario is played for first time. Consequently, new scenarios will be needed to get the most value from the game, which means there is a dependency on app development and maintenance support. Once that stops, the game will be disposable. This is a fundamental problem of games requiring software application support: the game is no longer self-contained; hence, there is an element of built-in obsolescence. Will this game still be playable in 10 or 20 years? Probably not. I have games in my collection that are 50 years old and still played. This is not going to be true for Mansion of Madness, 2nd Ed, but that doesn't mean the game is not a good value now.
• I'm not a fan of the monster minis. The minis add cost and take up storage space. We only use the monster tiles. This, of course, is just personal preference like most of the cons I've listed here.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
SO MUCH FUN!!!!  Wasn't sure what would happen next, very thematic, totally absorbing.  The main drawback though is that the play length is REALLY LONG for us.

Replay value is kinda iffy with just the base game.  From what I have seen (I started playing the same senario about 2 times before official game) there is not a whole lot of variety between plays.  Same stuff, same monsters, roughly the same places.  Rooms might change sides and the random rewards might be different but everything else is mostly the same.  In order to improve replay, you need to get more expansions for the app to pull items, monsters, and rooms from.

[b]Update:[/b] The expansions give the game app more choices when setting up tiles and such for each scenario.  Most of the key items of interest will still be there but some of the room layouts will change.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A huge improvement over the first ed, this is THE best game for those looking for a story-based "dungeon" crawl a la Betrayal at House on the Hill. Good mix of exploration, fighting monsters, solving puzzles, the app does a brilliant job at removing the need for a GM/overlord and controlling the "randomness" factor in story-based games by still making each play through of the same story somewhat variable but not placing you at the mercy of the random shuffle of a deck like BaHotH. I really like the test rolls where you don't know what the outcome will be - it's not simply succeed/fail, instead you might get 2 success rolls and receive a reward, but you don't know if 3 or 4 success rolls would have given a better reward. There are still some issues - since only one player can use the app at a time, even when they're reading out loud there can be some down time. Set-up is much faster than 1st ed, but the game still takes a very long time for a not-very-deep coop, and while you can save, clean-up, and continue another night, this is not ideal.  While I prefer sitting down at a table with friends over playing online games, this game is probably the perfect choice to be implemented on the digital tapletop device that's like a big iPad and dump the physical components, no sorting through tiles and minis to find the right one, track character location and status so horror checks and effects are automatic, etc.  Hard to say if it's worth the price though - I personally wouldn't buy it, but am happy to play a friend's copy.

RATING: 9
Betrayal at House on the Hill is probably my favorite board game, its the game that got me into board gaming as a hobby. That said, Mansions of Madness 2nd Edition is like the next logical evolution of Betrayal. It takes the "randomly made story" of Betrayal and turns it into this cohesive experience that feels almost closer to a video game than a board game. Its easy to teach because the app does most of the hard work, and its engaging the whole way through. I guess my only gripe is if you fail a story, you may have a hard time convincing your friends to replay that same story over. Luckily the game has plenty of stories and even DLC in the app to keep the game fresh, if they don't want to replay an old one, play a brand new one! 

I have painted all my miniatures from this game.

RATING: 6
Rating after 2 games, playing first scenario twice. The game is good and the atmosphere is thick. However, the minis are absolutely awful, ruining the first impression and there doesn't seem to be enough variety to warrant repeated playthroughs of the same scenario. EDIT: Lowered from 7 to 6 after another play, this time scenario 2. The app severely limits how the enemies behave and not in a good way. The mob enemy in scenario 2 is comically scripted to the point where you can chase them around rather than the opposite. The app quickly becomes annoying, unless you have a device with a large display (I used my 9 inch Kindle Fire HD which is the biggest portable device I have) you can barely see what's on the screen, they should have really invested in the lector for all the lines. And then there's bugs. Oh, how I missed the bugs from video gaming. Yesterday our Horror check read: WATER_SPLASH_02. That's it. This game combines the worst of both worlds, the fiddliness and simplistic enemy AI of board games and the neverending production cycle and rushed nature of modern video games. I won't be buying any more board games requiring and app and I won't be buying FFG games without doing a massive research on the component quality, which is a real shame.

RATING: 7.6
Incredibly immersive. People really aren't kidding when they say that the app really sets the mood for the game. It also handles all the bookkeeping and allows the players to all get into the game itself. Really easy to learn too.

Three negatives. One - There is no feedback from the app when a monster is defeated and it's a bit of a downer. The battles with monsters are epic and have severe consequences. So when you defeat one, you sort of expect a monster's dying scream or at the very least a message that says "Monster Defeated" but there isn't anything. Two - In typical Fantasy Flight fashion, the box insert is absolute garbage, but that's almost to be expected. It just sucks because there are so many miniatures that need to be stacked on top of each other. Three - Not enough campaigns for the price. It's still worth it to me, just because I love games like this, but it's borderline.

I know it seems like a small thing, but honestly, if the monster defeat thing was added, this game would be a 10 for me.

I have mixed feelings about this.  It solves the two biggest problems with first edition: long set up time, combined with the fact that a tiny and easily made mistake could break the game.

I'm fine with one against many and co-op games so that is a wash.

The down side is that so much has been shifted to the app, that it feels as much like a video game as a board game.  Also a great deal of the mechanics are buried in the app, which makes it feel a bit passive and gives it a bit of a feel of just bumbling along to see what happens.

In general, it makes it much more of a lighter game experience, but a much simpler and easier game.  Turns it into a gateway ameritrash storyteller more or less.

RATING: 7
I had a lot of fun with this, but I think it has two problems.  Many of your turns you will spend doing nothing but moving, and that is uninteresting.  With 5 players it can be boredom inducing.

The other main issue for me is that it claims to be fully cooperative, but it isn't.  You can 'win' by leaving everyone else behind, but there are no mechanics to cover it.  How does it follow that you can lose sanity and cause yourself mental problems throughout the game, but leaving everyone you were with to die for no reason is totally fine?  You even apparently have a psychic link with these people, given the game's mechanics.  It's not a problem when they die horrifically?  I think the semi-cooperative aspects could have been thought through better. 

Still, the app is great, and the one story we played through was involving enough with some nice little surprises.  I'm happy to play more, but I wouldn't be surprised if the problems grate on me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
First things first, this is an experience game, and it has very little replay value, even with new groups where a single player has played before. The app provides a very slick interface and makes the game run smoothly, providing you don't encounter any bugs (unlikely).

For your $120 CDN, you get a massive box which is half-filled with air and components you can only use if you owned the First Edition of this game. That's right, every monster tile that doesn't slide into a base (I say slide as if you didn't have to force them in) is for a game you probably don't own. The game also comes with replacement character cards for First Edition as well.

What you _will_ use are the giant, beautiful room tiles, 32 plastic figures of fairly shoddy quality, stands for those figures, monster tiles, and a ton of tokens. There are also some decks of cards on extremely good-quality stock.

The plastic monster stands just baffle me. The figures are not great, and you will probably need boiling water to fix one of the Star Spawn figures to fit in the base. The stands obscure the art on the tiles and expose only those bits of information you need. But the illustrations are scarier than the figures! This game could cost a good deal less if they'd just let you use the monster tiles without plastic. And since the tiles barely fit in the stands (the smallest ones, that is), I can't even get them back out without ruining them!

The game itself is fun, but has next to no replay value. You get four (FOUR!) scenarios with the app, and have to pay $5 or so for a fifth scenario. Buying a $40 expansion will get you two more scenarios, and each $60+ expansion gets you one (ONE!) more scenario. The value proposition on this game is terrible.

If FFG would continue pumping out IAP scenarios for this game like they did once before, I would say go for it, but they haven't done that yet.

UPDATE: Some of the Insanity cards are complete bullshit. You could be four hours into a scenario and Rita Young goes insane (she only has 5 Sanity and there may randomly be no way to cure her) and gets a card that sinks your entire "investigation". No fucking thanks. We removed the 3+ Insanity cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is kind of like an RPG, but an app plays the DM.  As a long time D&D player and a big fan of co-ops, I was immediately drawn to this concept.

The app is very well done, providing variable scenario setups, great flavor text, and theme music.  One thing that I really like is that it often throws skill tests at you, but doesn't tell you how many successes you need.  But, you still need to decide how many resources you want to use to maximize your successes.  There are a lot of characters an a lot of items that make the game replayable.  

I also really love how they did the spells.  When you gain the ability to cast a spell, you basically take a deck of 5 (or 4?) copies of the spell card.  On the back of each card, there is a different side effect that triggers when you cast it.  So, you never know exactly what is going to happen when you cast a spell.  I just really love the flavor of that.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've been waiting for a good game to come along and integrate an app properly, and this game seems to have finally done it.

To me it actually feels a lot like TIME Stories (TS), but the app handles all of the fiddly parts and puzzles. In fact I don't see a real need to play any more TS as long as they keep making new scenarios for this game.

At the basic level, MoM and TS both have an overarching story and you have to travel around and interact with different parts of the game in order to progress. MoM is less of a chore to play because the app handles a lot of the bad parts of TS. You don't have to dig through a deck of cards and put out certain ones in order to travel, you just click the screen in MoM. In TS you really had to take notes to play effectively, but MoM keeps track of everything automatically on the app. The puzzles also work better in MoM because you just slide things around on the screen.

I also feel like MoM could mostly replace Eldritch/Arkham Horror, which is too bad for me because I own almost everything for Eldritch. The main difference being that Eldritch has you travelling around the world and MoM has you exploring usually a single location, so the scope of the games feels different even the gameplay is very similar.

My only real complaint with MoM so far is with the miniatures and their bases. The bases themselves are these ugly, needlessly large, black plastic masses. The minis just have one little part that attaches into the base, so they don't stay in permanently. If anything ever touches it then the mini just pops out of the base and you have to fight to get it back into place. It will be almost essential to glue them into place.

Overall, a really good experience and a well done app.

Rating could go up or down after playing more scenarios.

RATING: 5
I think the game deserves praise for the theme and app integration, which I think are pretty excellent. The game itself, however, is kind of dull. Dice roll, dice roll, dice roll, dice roll... I once fancied myself a bit of an Ameri-trash fan, but I look at a game like this, where you need certain items / spells to mitigate negative outcomes without any recourse of acquiring them without complete dumb luck, and I'm really put off by how mechanically substandard this game is. I mean, it is in the same vein as, say, Eldritch Horror and Elder Sign (both of which I like), but in those games, you know where to go to acquire certain types of items. MoM is a complete crapshoot. You might get something to remove horror or you might eat a ton of it and go insane (or lose the game entirely) without having any real control over the outcome. There aren't any difficult decisions to make here; the game feels largely on rails.

RATING: 1
First of all I am not against apps in board games. I loved the way Alchemists used the app, and looking forward on how 	
First Martians: Adventures on the Red Planet and Rising 5 will use it.

However, the problem with MoM's design, is that there is no real need for a physical component. The app could have handled the character movement, the rolls, the conditions.

It feels odd and fiddly to select the monster that I want to attack on the app, choose how I attack it on the app, roll the physical dice, and input the result on the app? Why? Physically rolling the dice, and physically moving the plastic dolls around the map dose nothing for the gameplay, on the contrary it just creates disconnection, as you keep switching context.

Anyway, if you are running a Cthulhu RPG, than yes, the package is worth the money: you get some nice tiles and some nice plastic dolls, and as a bonus you get a decent board game, however if you are a board gamer, why on earth would you spend 100$ on a glorified app? 

In the end there are some things that video games(and maybe rpgs) will always do better(theme, immersion), while board are better when it comes to creating interesting situations with tough choices. I think designers should understand that, and play to the boardgame's strength, rather than trying to do everything.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I'm a technophile, so the app integration makes this extra cool for me!

The imagery and originality of the text that emerges during gameplay is literally better than any tabletop RPG that I've been in.

RATING: 8
About [thing=205059][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=205059][/thing] is the follow-up to [thing=83330][/thing], and it remains a game of horror, mystery and investigation in a very personal and small frame: usually a mansion or a manor. But it has two major changes: it is fully cooperative (well, at least for the majority of the time) and is app-driven.

The full cooperative and app-driven changes made possible for [thing=205059][/thing] be a good option for solo play, while in the first edition, this would only work in a few scenarios - none in the base game.

And it also made possible for the game to retain the mystery aspect of uncovering clues and unfold an investigation without the need of getting everything ready and in the proper place ahead of the time, and took out the drama of one mistake making the whole play impossible to finish, which is a major bummer when we are talking about a 2-5 hours game. This all means the game comes with a lot less cards (no more Keeper cards nor combat), and the set up is basically sorting some decks and tokens, picking investigators and scenario and went for it: a 10-15 minutes affair instead of the brutal time the first edition had.

Replay value seems higher, as the app can generate different configurations for the same mystery. 

The 2ed kept, however, the quality in art and miniatures, and the box is even bigger than before - most in the count of the Star Spawn miniatures.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
Much like in the 1st edition. Players will try to solve a mystery by going to a dreadful place. Every person has two actions, and the main actions possible by the investigators are:
- move up to 2 spots;
- search;
- fight;
- take items;
- trade.

Depending on the action done the app will call for some test. Dice are rolled and they are all d8, with some sides being success (elder sign), possible success (clues) and failure (blank). Player can turn the possible success into full success by spending clues, on a 1 to 1 ratio.

As before, the spells are double-sided, and once used, they can have some unexpected effects. Damage is handled different now: there are, still, Stamina and Sanity, but when either is damaged, players take cards: some will go facedown (just marking damage as if counters) and others face up, having constant ill effects. Is possible that, during the play, sometimes the app will call for the player to turn over one of her damage cards - the effect on the other side will be applied then. This is highly thematic, as old wounds open and come back to hinder again, and passsed traumas surface to make the person relive traumatic experiences.

If an investigators gets insane, the player takes one insanity card, which might add or change the way that investigator will win the scenario, usually giving the investigator something to do, even to the point of becoming a traitor.

Once everyone has had their turn, the Mythos phase come in. Monster will move and attack, if they can, some random or scenario-based events will take place, sanity will be rolled for those facing creatures, and so on.

Then is back to the investigators. Play will continue until the mystery is solved inside the time available, or until things end up in failure.

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
Which action to do. Normally the state of the game will show what is more pressing. Some guesswork might be necessary in order to find the evidences, or spots to be, or what the investigators truly need to do in order to win. But the scenario will provide clues in order to lead the investigators to the objective.

Normally the decisions will be group-based: "I do this, you do that, she goes there" and so on. Positioning and proper teamwork will be keys to win, regardless of the scenario.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Great production value, from art, to components and graphics;
- Strong thematic experience;
- Due to the unknown results of many actions, an alpha player won't have an easy time here;
- Set up and tear down aren't big issues, specially considering the "size" of the game;
- Downtime is less of an issue as it was in 1st edition (specially when considering combat and the Keeper phases);
- No more "I will always be the Keeper" thing.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- Is app-driven (hey, is my opinion and I don't like app in my analogic games);
- Replay value, while much less of an issue than before, is, still, an issue;
- Less fearful for the investigators than its predecessor.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Begrudgingly. I'm not a fan of apps in games. The less they do the more I like. Still, after going with my cane towards the ipad to say for it get out of my lawn, I must admit that the game was enjoyable and I wasn't as bothered as I thought I would. For a game that part A must happen before B and this one before C, otherwise things won't work, a computer program will take care of it better than humans in a hurry of playing already. An app was a good decision - it could be handled some other way? Probably, but the digital do allow for a type of freedom to add and change that the analog simply doesn't.

Now, does the second edition has a weaker parts when compared with the 1st? Yes, a few. The presence of a Keeper means the actions done to the investigators tend to be more focused, as in the 2ed, since the app doesn't take into the consideration the place the investigators are nor which actions was done by who, it means that the occasional event targeting someone happens by chance.

Also, a Keeper brings more tension to the game: the actions of the investigators tend to be done with one eye on the board and the other on the Keeper. "Will let me move freely? Will he use one of her cards now? What devilry is she planning?", and so on. As the AI is only activated when "touched", several actions are "safe" - in most, almost all cooperatives, this isn't an issue, but when you get in a terrible place, in a personal level, one would expect a constant feeling of tension and fear, and in MoM 2ed, this simpy isn't there.

Still, in the end, the 2ed improved the gameplay and easiness to get into the meat of the play. It isn't without flaws nor weak points when compared to the 1st ed, but when considered which one people will be playing more often and won't be scared of actually playing (mainly the Keeper), the 2ed is the clear winner.

RATING: 4.5
Based on 1 solo play of Cycle of Eternity which I won playing as 2 investigators (though I doubt more plays with more people would change my opinion).

I'm a little conflicted about this game, so I will try to summarize what I liked and what I disliked. But first of all let's talk about the app. Well, the app handles almost everything for you, except moving around and finding/drawing cards from their decks. And I guess that's fine, because it would require an enourmous amount of cards, tokens and bookkeeping if there were no app. I also liked the puzzles, even though the ones I came across were way too easy. My problems with the game are not about the app per se. 

+ The artwork is nice 
+ In theory I like the aspect of upgrading your investigator through items and spells (even though you have little control over what you are getting, basically just a few hints from the app/tile. I mean you are not gonna find a sledgehammer in a pile of papers..)
+ When you start playing for the first time, the first 20 minutes or so feels amazing. It feels like you have so much to explore and solve. 
+ It's an awesome premise. Horror, mystery, investigations. If this game was mechanically fun and interesting, it could have been love. 

- It such a passive experience. Not necessarily because of the app, but because the outcome of the decisions you make are always based on some random diceroll, or other randomness. 

- The few decisions you make in the game are obvious. Do I search this search icon that are in the same space as I? Of course I do, what else am I gonna do in here! 

- Did I mention the dice rolls? You better like it, because you are gonna roll A LOT of them. Search the cabinet - roll the dice, attack the monster - roll the dice, evade the monster - roll the dice, talk to a person - roll the dice, monster attacks - roll the dice, monster eats an eel - roll the freaking dice again. 

- The minis are bad quality and the mini bases are annoying. Eventually I just used the monster tokens on the tiles. 

- Ok I only played one scenario, but half way through I didn't care for the story anymore. I'm not a Lovecraftian hardcore fan, but I have enjoyed other Cthulhu games. This game rely a lot on it's storytelling, but it didn't work for me. 

- I didn't feel that I was actually "solving" anything by the end of it. 

- Most items and spells you get feels random, and more often than not end up being almost totally useless for your investigator/situation. 

- Even though the app handles stuff for you, I think I would rather go for Eldritch Horror than this. In EH you at least keep your mind occupied all the time, since there is no app that's is telling you what is happening. 

I am not sure yet if I will go this another go, or just get it out the door now. Right now I am not very tempted to try again.

EDIT: Sold my copy of the game
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I am very torn on this one. It is undoubtedly cool, and probably as a design even better than 1st Edition MoM.
BUT, I wholeheartedly agree with the ones that say that, in this alleged BOARD-game, there is no actual need for the board, nor the cards, miniatures or anything at all that isn't the app itself. Actually I think that the switching between the app and the physical components detracts from, and not adds to, the experience.
So, as a boardgame I find it lacking. As a roleplaying experience, even if the scenarios are well done, MoM doesn't cut it compared to "real" pen-and-paper RPGs. In the end, it is not a game that I could recommend, particularly at this price tag.

RATING: 8
Great game for those who enoyed betrayal at house on the hill, featuring tile-based exploration this game is more focused on story driven investigation of the world around you based on a chosen scenario,

The large tiles feature multiple spaces - each of which can have searchable features within to discover hints at how to foil the evil goings on and who is purpotrating them or explore markers to reveal a new tile of the mansion/town/forest and it's contents. Each time you want to interact with something you just tap it's corresponding symbol on the app map and it explains what you find and how you can interact with it.

Gameplay is relatively simply and quick to pick up with 2 actions per investigator then swapping over to the monster or Mythos phase where something weird happens and enemies move, attack and cause psychological horror to the investigators.

The whole game is also driven by the App, the first edition required a player to determine which tiles went where, what monster spawned etc. in a more formal pen and paper RPG role while now all of that is left to the app and you can enjoy the game single player or co-op with 4 friends as well as yourself. 

Miniatures can be painted if you're into that scene or simply used as-is. The models themselves look great and come on firm bases that the monster cards can slot into exposing their key stats for reference (if space is an issue you can always just use the monster cards alone).

Games range from 1-6 hours depending how in-depth of an investigation you want to do, exploring just a single mansion with a self-contained plot, or exploring each district of a famous port town over several days discovering what evil plans are afoot and who is responsible. The objective is usually to stop who is responsible by exploring and discovering involvement and evidence cards or survive the reprecussions of failing to prevent something happening which can lead to a violent showdown.

Losing an investigator does only leave you with one turn left to finish the game, so keeping people alive is a high priorty. Taking your max health in damage or max sanity in horror damage will result in a permanent effect for the rest of the game - broken legs that reduce movement, or insanity effects only that player is allowed to know that can turn them into enemies on the sly or alter the success conditions.

Every game is randomised where possible - mostly in the monsters that spawn, mythos events, horror descriptors and skill check flavour texts. Layouts of the scenario being played can vary between a few different designs and those can be varied further with more tiles for the game to work with. Core elements of each scenario will remain the same as the antagonists and their plans are consistent but the App will prevent you solving the mystery right away if you don't have a certain key or evidence for your investigator to know where to go or who to apprehend.

Ultimately there is still room for replayability both solo and with friends and the expansions reinforce that. There are additional scenarios for each of the expansions (the first edition re-box Reoccuring Nightmares and the first edition expansions re-box Suppressed Memories) and one additional scenario for use with the 2nd edition parts as a DLC.

With another expansion specifically for 2nd Ed. called 'Beyond the Threshold' coming this year it looks like the game has only just begun its lifecycle and has plenty of potential to offer.

Update as of 9/20/17: I have downgraded 2nd ed from a 7 to a 6. I have tried to pinpoint what irked me about this design. At first I thought it was the Insane condition cards (which I very strongly dislike) but that wasn't the main issue. Recently, we played a scenario with an awesome story and sweet mystery. We played and discovered so much, and the game shifted into the second half. Monsters spawned and started attacking an NPC, but they never dealt any damage. They just stalled out the NPC. We proceeded to run up and just roll the dice until we got enough success to kill them and then move on with the interesting story.

This part of rolling, rolling, and rolling dice is boring. It takes away the immersion. "Fortunately" we were able to roll enough successes in order to win, but by that point, we just didn't care any more. I do think you should play this game if you are interested, but I would love to never have to play again. Oddly enough, maybe I now have a decent reason to return to first edition.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Mansions of Madness has always been a "once or twice per year" game for my group, and the second edition doesn't change that.  Others in the same category:  Android, Legend of the Moonsword, Betrayal at House on the Hill, Doom.

The original was reasonably priced, but was a bear to set up.  The new one is the bizarro anti-version: set-up is now negligible, but you have to lay down a lot more cash to ride.  The stiff price hike means that I can't justify grabbing the update, particularly since set-up doesn't matter when you rarely play.

Content-wise, the ambient music in version 2 is cool.  But otherwise the app seems malnourished.  Minimally, each scenario deserves event-based sound effects - where's that music box you keep mentioning? - and a half-dozen more voiceovers. For an app game, there's an awful lot of read-aloud by the players.  And it wouldn't hurt for the evasion and attack text to be repeated far less often.

Ironically, my biggest problem with MoM v2 is that, electronics aside, it's just too similar to the original.  The game could benefit from new and exciting app-supported mechanics that shake things up.

Like what exactly?  I'm not sure.  Secret mumblings for insane players, maybe. Event descriptions that change, depending on your character's location. Random jump scares.  An occasional dexterity aspect to failed combat rolls. Angels with dirty faces. I dunno, just something. 

I do like the app-based puzzles, though.  Good call.

RATING: 4
The app provides an atmospheric backdrop to the game with the music and narrative it also manages a large amount of the book keeping speeding set up time up significantly. The game is very dice dependent and luck driven and it can be long too (sometimes too long). There will be times where after several hours of play the game will be decided not by how well you've planned but by the roll of the dice. After two plays I'm looking forward to exploring more of the investigations but I have my reservations about the luck factor in a game of this length.

This is the first board game I have bought that I keenly felt like I paid too much for it. The high price point is not really justified - huge amounts of the art are reused from other titles and the miniatures are substandard compared to other FFG products (Imperial Assault). The app would have cost for development though and I think that the app is very strong so there is that - I guess I just don't appreciate that so much since it is not tangible in the same way that the miniatures are.

It is a fun and immersive game and ultimately that's what counts. I need to get in several more plays before my evaluation matures but I'm starting this at an 8.
 I have dropped my rating of this considerably since I feel like it is far too random and far too long - You can invest upwards of three hours in a story and then lose to arbitrary damage/ horror hand outs and bad dice rolls. I wouldn't mind the random elements if the game was shorter and there was less emphasis on the story.

Wow, after a long break from the game I have tried it again and really loathed it. It felt like the game was intentionally designed to waste time and obfuscate interesting choices in needless layers of nonsense. The actions are limited the time spent clicking through the app and moving everything procedural;y is unforgivable the combat is dreadful and the atmosphere is short lived as it gets bogged down in the mire of fiddling around with the pieces. Poor die rolls are crippling, the health and sanity cards lead to further time wasting. For example I had to flip a sanity card that made me drop two items, then my character got stunned which meant they could only perform one action. Therefore as my only action I could pick up the items I dropped and my turn would be over. Now we go through the entire charade of moving monsters about and rolling horror checks again so I can have two more actions. So uninteresting, what a waste of an afternoon.

RATING: 2.3
First game I have ever played with an app tied into it. I would hope this is the worst of these.

Seperately the board game on its own if you replaced the app aspects with cards would also have been a very bad game. Decisions on what you did with your character don't mean much since you don't know what aspect of your character will be used for any action and there isn't a mechanic that tells you ahead of time in too many cases.

If it was just an app and all the board game aspects were managed in app, that app would be even worse.  Feels like one of the crappier games you would have played back in the Windows 3.1 days. Just background mood music that you would think would have sound effects or change with events but it doesn't. Text to read about what randomly happened like reading from an event card pile.  If you click continue there is no going back and reading what you missed or correcting a mistake.

The mix of the 2 together I think makes both somehow worse. Things that seemed like it should be handled in app it had you handle on your own and vice versa.  The biggest downfall of it though is the having the app be the focal point of everything took away from the community interaction feel of a boardgame.

I would hope this is just one of the worst implementation of an app into a game.  It seems like as just a small mechanism would work much better, but maybe this is just a short terrible trend like VCR board games.

That all being said this is clearly a game not built for me.  Others I played with seem to enjoy it more.  I can enjoy cooperative and/or thematic games, but I tend to enjoy them less.  This one is the least enjoyable modern game I have played so far.
